900 <h2>

970<section id="_607">

971<h3 id="_607_toc">

972World Health Organization (WHO) Classification for CNS Embryonal Tumors and Pineoblastoma</h3>

973<p id="_608" tabindex="-1">

974 Embryonal tumors are a collection of biologically heterogeneous lesions that share the tendency to disseminate throughout the nervous system via cerebrospinal fluid (CSF) pathways. Although there is significant variability, histologically these tumors are grouped together because they are at least partially composed of hyperchromatic cells (blue cell tumors on standard staining) with little cytoplasm, which are densely packed and demonstrate a high degree of mitotic activity. Other histological and immunohistochemical features, such as the degree of apparent cellular transformation along identifiable cell lineages (ependymal, glial, etc.), can be used to separate these tumors to some degree. However, a convention, which has been accepted by the WHO, also separates these tumors on the basis of presumed location of origin within the central nervous system (CNS). Molecular studies have substantiated the differences between tumors arising in different areas of the brain and give partial credence to this classification approach.[<a href="#cit/section_1.1">

9751</a>

976]</p>

977<p id="_1796" tabindex="-1">

978 As of 2016, the WHO has proposed an integrated phenotypic and genotypic classification system for CNS tumors in which diagnoses are layered with WHO grade, histological classification, and molecular classification.[<a href="#cit/section_1.2">

9792</a>

980] The term primitive neuroectodermal tumor (PNET) has been removed from the WHO diagnostic lexicon, although some rare entities (e.g., medulloepithelioma) have remained. A molecularly distinct entity, embryonal tumor with multilayered rosettes (ETMR), <em class="gene-name">

981C19MC</em>

982-altered, was added, encompassing embryonal tumor with abundant neuropil and true rosettes (ETANTR), ependymoblastoma, and medulloepithelioma. The WHO classification will be updated as other molecularly distinct entities are defined.</p>

983<p id="_614" tabindex="-1">

984The pathological diagnosis of embryonal tumors is based primarily on histological and immunohistological microscopic features. However, molecular genetic studies are employed increasingly to subclassify embryonal tumors. These molecular genetic findings are also being used for risk stratification and treatment planning.[<a href="#cit/section_1.3">

9853</a>

986-<a href="#cit/section_1.6">

9876</a>

988]</p>

989<p id="_609" tabindex="-1">

990The 2021 WHO classification of embryonal tumors is as follows:[<a href="#cit/section_1.7">

9917</a>

992,<a href="#cit/section_1.8">

9938</a>

994] </p>

995<div class="pdq-content-list">

996<ul id="_2100">

997<li>

998<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-cns-embryonal-treatment-pdq#_1896">

999Medulloblastoma</a>

1000.<div class="pdq-content-list">

1001<ul id="_2101">

1002<li>

1003Medulloblastomas, molecularly defined.<div class="pdq-content-list">

1004<ul id="_2102">

1005<li>

1006Medulloblastoma, WNT-activated.</li>

1007<li>

1008Medulloblastoma, SHH-activated and <em class="gene-name">

1009TP53</em>

1010-wild type.</li>

1011<li>

1012Medulloblastoma, SHH-activated and <em class="gene-name">

1013TP53</em>

1014-mutant.</li>

1015<li>

1016Medulloblastoma, non-WNT/non-SHH.</li>

1017</ul>

1018</div>

1019</li>

1020<li>

1021Medulloblastomas, histologically defined.<div class="pdq-content-list">

1022<ul id="_2117">

1023<li>

1024Desmoplastic nodular medulloblastoma.</li>

1025<li>

1026Medulloblastoma with extensive nodularity.</li>

1027<li>

1028Large cell medulloblastoma.</li>

1029<li>

1030Anaplastic medulloblastoma.</li>

1031</ul>

1032</div>

1033</li>

1034</ul>

1035</div>

1036</li>

1037<li>

1038Other CNS embryonal tumors.<div class="pdq-content-list">

1039<ul id="_2103">

1040<li>

1041Atypical teratoid/rhabdoid tumor.</li>

1042<li>

1043Cribriform neuroepithelial tumor.</li>

1044<li>

1045Embryonal tumor with multilayered rosettes.</li>

1046<li>

1047CNS neuroblastoma, <em class="gene-name">

1048FOXR2</em>

1049-activated.</li>

1050<li>

1051CNS tumor with <em class="gene-name">

1052BCOR</em>

1053 internal tandem duplication.</li>

1054<li>

1055CNS embryonal tumor NEC/NOS.</li>

1056</ul>

1057</div>

1058</li>

1059</ul>

1060</div>

1061<p id="_615" tabindex="-1">

1062Pineoblastoma, which in the past was conventionally grouped with embryonal tumors, is categorized by the WHO as a pineal parenchymal tumor. The 2021 WHO classification of these tumors is as follows:[<a href="#cit/section_1.7">

10637</a>

1064,<a href="#cit/section_1.8">

10658</a>

1066] </p>

1067<div class="pdq-content-list">

1068<ul id="_2104">

1069<li>

1070Pineocytoma.</li>

1071<li>

1072Pineal parenchymal tumor of intermediate differentiation.</li>

1073<li>

1074Pineoblastoma.</li>

1075<li>

1076Papillary tumor of the pineal region.</li>

1077<li>

1078Desmoplastic myxoid tumor of the pineal region, <em class="gene-name">

1079SMARCB1</em>

1080-mutant.</li>

1081</ul>

1082</div>

1083<p id="_2105" tabindex="-1">

1084Given that therapies for pineoblastomas are quite similar to those for embryonal tumors, pineoblastomas are discussed in this summary. A somewhat closely aligned tumor, pineal parenchymal tumor of intermediate differentiation, has been identified but is not considered an embryonal tumor and primarily arises in adults.[<a href="#cit/section_1.2">

10852</a>

1086]</p>

1087</section>

1088<section id="_1984">

1089<h3 id="_1984_toc">

1090Anatomy</h3>

1091<p id="_548" tabindex="-1">

1092<figure id="figure_549" class="image-center">

1093<a href="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/748659.jpg" target="_blank" class="article-image-enlarge">

1094Enlarge</a>

1095<img id="_549" alt="Drawing of the inside of the brain showing the supratentorial area (the upper part of the brain) and the posterior fossa/infratentorial area (the lower back part of the brain). The supratentorial area contains the cerebrum, lateral ventricle, third ventricle, choroid plexus, hypothalamus, pineal gland, pituitary gland, and optic nerve. The posterior fossa/infratentorial area contains the cerebellum, tectum, fourth ventricle, and brain stem (pons and medulla). The tentorium and spinal cord are also shown." title="Drawing of the inside of the brain showing the supratentorial area (the upper part of the brain) and the posterior fossa/infratentorial area (the lower back part of the brain). The supratentorial area contains the cerebrum, lateral ventricle, third ventricle, choroid plexus, hypothalamus, pineal gland, pituitary gland, and optic nerve. The posterior fossa/infratentorial area contains the cerebellum, tectum, fourth ventricle, and brain stem (pons and medulla). The tentorium and spinal cord are also shown." src="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/748659-750.jpg">

1096<figcaption class="caption-container">

1097Figure 1. Anatomy of the inside of the brain, showing the pineal and pituitary glands, optic nerve, ventricles (with cerebrospinal fluid shown in blue), and other parts of the brain. The posterior fossa is the region below the tentorium, which separates the cortex from the cerebellum and essentially denotes the region containing the brain stem, cerebellum, and fourth ventricle.</figcaption>

1098</figure>

1099</p>

1100</section>

1101<section id="_541">

1102<h3 id="_541_toc">

1103Incidence</h3>

1104<p id="_542" tabindex="-1">

1105Embryonal tumors account for 20% to 25% of primary CNS tumors (malignant brain tumors and pilocytic astrocytomas) arising in children. These tumors occur along the pediatric age spectrum but tend to cluster early in life. The incidence of embryonal tumors in children aged 1 to 9 years is fivefold to tenfold higher than in adults (see <a href="/types/brain/hp/child-cns-embryonal-treatment-pdq#_662">

1106Table 1</a>

1107).[<a href="#cit/section_1.9">

11089</a>

1109,<a href="#cit/section_1.10">

111010</a>

1111] </p>

1112<table id="_662" class="table-default expandable-container">

1113<caption>

1114Table 1. Annual Incidence Rates for Childhood Central Nervous System Embryonal Tumors According to Age<span class="sup">

1115a</span>

1116</caption>

1117<colgroup>

1118<col width="50.00%">

1119<col width="50.00%">

1120</colgroup>

1121<THead>

1122<tr>

1123<th>

1124Age Group (y) </th>

1125<th>

1126Annual Incidence Rate (Cases per 1 Million)</th>

1127</tr>

1128</THead>

1129<TFoot class="pdq-footer">

1130<tr>

1131<td colspan="2">

1132<span class="sup">

1133a</span>

1134Source: Childhood cancer by the International Classification of Childhood Cancer [<a href="#cit/section_1.9">

11359</a>

1136] and Smoll et al.[<a href="#cit/section_1.10">

113710</a>

1138]</td>

1139</tr>

1140</TFoot>

1141<tbody>

1142<tr>

1143<td>

1144&lt;5</td>

1145<td>

114611</td>

1147</tr>

1148<tr>

1149<td>

11505&#8211;9</td>

1151<td>

11527</td>

1153</tr>

115610&#8211;19</td>

11583&#8211;4</td>

1159</tr>

1160</tbody>

1161</table>

1162<p id="_632" tabindex="-1">

1163Medulloblastomas comprise the vast majority of pediatric embryonal tumors. By definition, they arise in the posterior fossa (see <a href="/types/brain/hp/child-cns-embryonal-treatment-pdq#_549">

1164Figure 1</a>

1165), where they constitute approximately 40% of all posterior fossa tumors. Other forms of embryonal tumors each make up 2% or less of all childhood brain tumors.</p>

1166</section>

1167<section id="_307">

1168<h3 id="_307_toc">

1169Diagnostic and Staging Evaluation</h3>

1170<p id="_1985" tabindex="-1">

1171Imaging studies and CSF analysis are included in the diagnostic and staging evaluation.</p>

1172<section id="_1986">

1173<h4 id="_1986_toc">

1174Imaging studies</h4>

1175<p id="_1987" tabindex="-1">

1176Diagnosis is usually made by either magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) or computed tomography (CT) scan. MRI is preferable because the anatomical relationship between the tumor and surrounding brain and tumor dissemination is better visualized with this method.[<a href="#cit/section_1.11">

117711</a>

1178]</p>

1179<p id="_635" tabindex="-1">

1180After diagnosis, evaluation of embryonal tumors is quite similar, essentially independent of the histological subtype and the location of the tumor. Given the tendency of these tumors to disseminate throughout the CNS early in the course of illness, imaging evaluation of the neuraxis by MRI of the entire brain and spine is indicated. Preferably, this is done before surgery to avoid postoperative artifacts, especially blood. Such imaging can be difficult to interpret and must be performed in at least two planes, with and without the use of contrast enhancement (gadolinium).[<a href="#cit/section_1.12">

118112</a>

1182] A study of the significance of equivocal findings on spinal MRIs in children with medulloblastoma identified equivocal findings in 48 of 100 patients (48%). The study reported the following results:[<a href="#cit/section_1.13">

118313</a>

1184] </p>

1185<div class="pdq-content-list">

1186<ul id="_1988">

1187<li>

1188Analysis by subgroup identified a higher proportion of equivocal findings in the SHH subgroup (<em>

1189P</em>

1190 = .007). </li>

1191<li>

1192The 5-year overall survival (OS) rate in children with equivocal MRI findings (80%) was not different from the 5-year OS in patients who had normal MRI findings (84.8%), while OS in patients with M3 metastases was worse (54.7%) (<em>

1193P</em>

1194 = .02).</li>

1195</ul>

1196</div>

1197<p id="_1989" tabindex="-1">

1198In contrast, a Children's Oncology Group (COG) prospective study treated over 400 children without metastatic disease with a reduced dose (23.4 Gy) of craniospinal radiation therapy. Nearly 20% of patients with central neuroradioagraphical review were found to have either evidence of possible excessive residual disease and/or metastatic disease or were considered to have imaging inadequate to fully evaluate the neuroaxis. For patients with centrally reviewed imaging, children considered to have metastatic disease had poor OS compared with those with nondisseminated disease. The subgroup found to have inadequate imaging by central review had an intermediate survival rate between the children with adequate imaging and those who had metastatic disease.[<a href="#cit/section_1.12">

119912</a>

1200] In a subsequent prospective COG study that treated over 500 children with reduced-dose craniospinal radiation therapy (23.4 Gy or 18 Gy), patients with inadequate imaging had poorer survival.[<a href="#cit/section_1.14">

120114</a>

1202] Consensus guidelines for timing and neuroimaging techniques have been recommended and include details that outline standards for preoperative assessment of the entire neuroaxis and postoperative assessment of amount of residual disease.[<a href="#cit/section_1.15">

120315</a>

1204]</p>

1205<p id="_1990" tabindex="-1">

1206After surgery, imaging of the primary tumor site is indicated to determine the extent of residual disease. </p>

1207</section>

1208<section id="_1991">

1209<h4 id="_1991_toc">

1210CSF analysis</h4>

1211<p id="_569" tabindex="-1">

1212After surgery, lumbar CSF analysis is performed, if deemed safe. Neuroimaging and CSF evaluation are considered complementary because as many as 10% of patients will have evidence of free-floating tumor cells in the CSF without clear evidence of leptomeningeal disease on MRI scan.[<a href="#cit/section_1.16">

121316</a>

1214] </p>

1215<p id="_1992" tabindex="-1">

1216CSF analysis is conventionally done 14 to 21 days after surgery. If CSF is obtained within 14 days of the operation, detection of tumor cells within the spinal fluid is possibly related to the surgical procedure. In most staging systems, if fluid is obtained in the first few days after surgery and found to be positive for tumor cells, the positivity must be confirmed by a subsequent spinal tap to be considered diagnostically significant. In contrast, if CSF is negative for tumor cells at that time, then no confirmation is needed. When obtainment of fluid by lumbar spinal tap is deemed unsafe, ventricular fluid can be obtained; however, this method may not be as sensitive as lumbar fluid assessment.[<a href="#cit/section_1.16">

121716</a>

1218]</p>

1219<p id="_627" tabindex="-1">

1220Because embryonal tumors are very rarely metastatic to the bone, bone marrow, or other body sites at the time of diagnosis, studies such as bone marrow aspirates, chest x-rays, or bone scans are not indicated, unless there are symptoms or signs suggesting organ involvement.</p>

1221</section>

1222</section>

1223<section id="_309">

1224<h3 id="_309_toc">

1225Prognostic Factors</h3>

1226<p id="_391" tabindex="-1">

1227Various clinical and biological parameters have been associated with the likelihood of disease control of embryonal tumors after treatment.[<a href="#cit/section_1.4">

12284</a>

1229] The significance of many of these factors have been shown to be predictive for medulloblastomas, although some are used to assign risk, to some degree, for other embryonal tumors. Parameters that are most frequently used to predict outcome include the following:[<a href="#cit/section_1.17">

123017</a>

1231,<a href="#cit/section_1.18">

123218</a>

1233]</p>

1234<div class="pdq-content-list">

1235<ul id="_414">

1236<li>

1237<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-cns-embryonal-treatment-pdq#_416">

1238Extent of CNS disease at diagnosis</a>

1239.</li>

1240<li>

1241<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-cns-embryonal-treatment-pdq#_419">

1242Age at diagnosis</a>

1243.</li>

1244<li>

1245<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-cns-embryonal-treatment-pdq#_576">

1246Amount of residual disease after definitive surgery</a>

1247.</li>

1248<li>

1249<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-cns-embryonal-treatment-pdq#_421">

1250Tumor histopathology</a>

1251.</li>

1252<li>

1253<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-cns-embryonal-treatment-pdq#_423">

1254Biological/molecular tumor cell characteristics</a>

1255.</li>

1256</ul>

1257</div>

1258<p id="_621" tabindex="-1">

1259It has become increasingly clear, especially for medulloblastomas, that outcome is also related to the molecular characteristics of the tumor, but this has not been definitively shown for other embryonal tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_1.1">

12601</a>

1261,<a href="#cit/section_1.5">

12625</a>

1263,<a href="#cit/section_1.6">

12646</a>

1265,<a href="#cit/section_1.19">

126619</a>

1267-<a href="#cit/section_1.21">

126821</a>

1269] OS rates range from 40% to 90%, depending on the molecular subtype of the medulloblastoma and possibly other factors, such as extent of dissemination at time of diagnosis and degree of resection. Children with medulloblastoma who survive for 5 years are considered cured of their tumor. Survival rates for other embryonal tumors are generally poorer, ranging from less than 5% to 50%. Specific survival rates are discussed within each subgroup in the summary.[<a href="#cit/section_1.22">

127022</a>

1271-<a href="#cit/section_1.25">

127225</a>

1273]</p>

1274<p id="_574" tabindex="-1">

1275In older studies, the presence of brain stem involvement in children with medulloblastoma was found to be a prognostic factor. It has not been found to be of predictive value in subsequent studies that treated patients with both radiation and chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_1.12">

127612</a>

1277,<a href="#cit/section_1.17">

127817</a>

1279]</p>

1280<p id="_1832" tabindex="-1">

1281An accurate diagnosis is critical for patients with embryonal tumors. For example, in the <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00392327">

1282ACNS0332 (NCT00392327)</a>

1283 trial that enrolled 80 patients with high-risk medulloblastoma, supratentorial CNS-PNET tumors, and pineoblastoma, 60 patients had sufficient tissue for evaluation. Thirty-one tumors were nonpineal in location, 22 (71%) of which represented tumors that were not intended for trial inclusion, including 18 high-grade gliomas, 2 atypical teratoid/rhabdoid tumors, and 2 ependymomas. Outcomes across tumor types were strikingly different. Patients with supratentorial embryonal tumors/pineoblastomas exhibited a 5-year event-free survival (EFS) rate of 62.8% (95% confidence interval [CI], 43.4%&#8211;82.2%) and an OS rate of 78.5% (95% CI, 62.2%&#8211;94.8%), whereas patients with molecularly classified high-grade gliomas had a 5-year EFS rate of 5.6% (95% CI, 0%&#8211;13%) and an OS rate of 12% (95% CI, 0%&#8211;24.7%). Survival rates for patients with high-grade gliomas were similar to those of patients who were enrolled in historical studies that avoided craniospinal irradiation and intensive chemotherapy. Thus, for patients with CNS-PNET/pineoblastoma, prognosis is considerably better than previously assumed when molecularly confirmed high-grade gliomas are removed.[<a href="#cit/section_1.26">

128426</a>

1285]</p>

1286<p id="_512" tabindex="-1">

1287Prognosis is poor for patients with medulloepithelioma and ETMR, with 5-year survival rates ranging between 0% and 30%.[<a href="#cit/section_1.27">

128827</a>

1289-<a href="#cit/section_1.29">

129029</a>

1291] In a retrospective multivariate analysis of 38 patients, total or near-total resection, the use of radiation therapy, and the use of high-dose chemotherapy were associated with an improved prognosis.[<a href="#cit/section_1.30">

129230</a>

1293][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1294Level of evidence C1</a>

1295]</p>

1296<section id="_416">

1297<h4 id="_416_toc">

1298Extent of CNS disease at diagnosis</h4>

1299<p id="_417" tabindex="-1">

1300Patients with disseminated CNS disease at diagnosis are at highest risk of disease relapse.[<a href="#cit/section_1.16">

130116</a>

1302-<a href="#cit/section_1.18">

130318</a>

1304] Ten percent to 40% of patients with medulloblastoma have CNS dissemination at diagnosis, with infants having the highest incidence and adolescents and adults having the lowest incidence.</p>

1305<p id="_418" tabindex="-1">

1306Nonmedulloblastoma embryonal tumors and pineoblastomas may also be disseminated at the time of diagnosis, although the incidence of dissemination may be somewhat less than that of medulloblastomas, with dissemination at diagnosis being documented in approximately 10% to 20% of patients.[<a href="#cit/section_1.22">

130722</a>

1308,<a href="#cit/section_1.23">

130923</a>

1310] Patients with nonmedulloblastoma embryonal tumors and pineoblastomas who have disseminated disease at the time of diagnosis have a poor OS, with reported survival rates at 5 years ranging from 10% to 30%.[<a href="#cit/section_1.22">

131122</a>

1312-<a href="#cit/section_1.25">

131325</a>

1314] </p>

1315</section>

1316<section id="_419">

1317<h4 id="_419_toc">

1318Age at diagnosis</h4>

1319<p id="_575" tabindex="-1">

1320Age younger than 3 years at diagnosis (except for desmoplastic medulloblastoma/medulloblastoma with extensive nodularity [MBEN]) portends an unfavorable outcome for those with medulloblastoma and, possibly, other embryonal tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_1.31">

132131</a>

1322-<a href="#cit/section_1.36">

132336</a>

1324]</p>

1325</section>

1326<section id="_576">

1327<h4 id="_576_toc">

1328Amount of residual disease after definitive surgery</h4>

1329<p id="_636" tabindex="-1">

1330As a predictor of outcome, postoperative MRI measurement of the amount of residual disease after definitive surgery has been supplanted by extent of resection after surgery.[<a href="#cit/section_1.12">

133112</a>

1332]</p>

1333<p id="_577" tabindex="-1">

1334In older studies, the extent of resection for medulloblastomas was found to be related to survival.[<a href="#cit/section_1.17">

133517</a>

1336,<a href="#cit/section_1.18">

133718</a>

1338,<a href="#cit/section_1.37">

133937</a>

1340,<a href="#cit/section_1.38">

134138</a>

1342] A Hirntumor and International Society of Paediatric Oncology study of 340 children reported that residual disease (&gt;1.5 cm<span class="sup">

13432</span>

1344) connoted a poorer 5-year EFS rate.[<a href="#cit/section_1.39">

134539</a>

1346] Extent of resection after surgery is still used to separate patients into risk groups, with patients having more than 1.5 cm<span class="sup">

13472</span>

1348 of residual disease stratified into high-risk groups, with intensification of craniospinal irradiation to 36 Gy. </p>

1349<p id="_1994" tabindex="-1">

1350An international, retrospective, collaborative study included 787 patients of all ages with medulloblastoma who were treated in a variety of ways and incorporated molecular subgrouping and clinical factors in the analysis. The multivariate analysis found that subtotal resection (&gt;1.5 cm<span class="sup">

13512</span>

1352 residual), but not near-total resection (&lt;1.5 cm<span class="sup">

13532</span>

1354 residual), was associated with inferior progression-free survival compared with gross-total resection. This study suggested that attempts to completely remove the tumor, especially when the likelihood of neurological morbidity is high, are not warranted because there appears to be little or no benefit to gross-total resection when compared with near-total resection. It gives some credence to the present approach in which patients with more than 1.5 cm<span class="sup">

13552</span>

1356 of disease are considered higher-risk patients.[<a href="#cit/section_1.40">

135740</a>

1358] Prospective studies are needed to better define the impact of extent of resection on outcome within molecularly defined subgroups.</p>

1359<p id="_578" tabindex="-1">

1360In patients with other forms of embryonal tumors, the extent of resection has not been definitively shown to impact survival.[<a href="#cit/section_1.24">

136124</a>

1362] However, in a COG study of 66 children with supratentorial embryonal tumors, extent of resection was found to be prognostic for those with localized disease at the time of diagnosis.[<a href="#cit/section_1.41">

136341</a>

1364]</p>

1365</section>

1366<section id="_421">

1367<h4 id="_421_toc">

1368Tumor histopathology</h4>

1369<p id="_422" tabindex="-1">

1370For medulloblastomas, histopathological features such as large cell variant, anaplasia, and desmoplasia have been shown in retrospective analyses to correlate with outcome.[<a href="#cit/section_1.32">

137132</a>

1372,<a href="#cit/section_1.42">

137342</a>

1374,<a href="#cit/section_1.43">

137543</a>

1376] In prospective studies, immunohistochemical and histopathological findings have not predicted outcome in children older than 3 years at diagnosis, with the exception of the large cell/anaplastic variant, which has been associated with poorer prognosis.[<a href="#cit/section_1.12">

137712</a>

1378,<a href="#cit/section_1.21">

137921</a>

1380,<a href="#cit/section_1.44">

138144</a>

1382] Several studies have observed that the histological finding of desmoplasia, seen in patients aged 3 years and younger with desmoplastic medulloblastoma, especially MBEN, connotes a significantly better prognosis compared with outcomes for infants and young children with classic or large cell/anaplastic medulloblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_1.21">

138321</a>

1384,<a href="#cit/section_1.31">

138531</a>

1386-<a href="#cit/section_1.33">

138733</a>

1388,<a href="#cit/section_1.45">

138945</a>

1390]; [<a href="#cit/section_1.34">

139134</a>

1392][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1393Level of evidence B4</a>

1394] </p>

1395<p id="_628" tabindex="-1">

1396For other embryonal tumors, histological variations have not been associated with differing outcome.</p>

1397</section>

1398<section id="_423">

1399<h4 id="_423_toc">

1400Biological/molecular tumor cell characteristics</h4>

1401<section id="_2118">

1402<h5 id="_2118_toc">

1403Measure of minimal residual disease</h5>

1404<p id="_2119" tabindex="-1">

1405In one study, CSF copy number variations, similar to those found in the primary tumors, were prognostic of relapse when present after radiation therapy or during or after chemotherapy. If this finding is replicated in prospective clinical trials and the technique becomes available, it will be an important measure of minimal residual disease and likely will become part of the baseline evaluation, as well as part of surveillance testing.[<a href="#cit/section_1.46">

140646</a>

1407]</p>

1408</section>

1409<section id="_2120">

1410<h5 id="_2120_toc">

1411Genomic analyses</h5>

1412<p id="_617" tabindex="-1">

1413Genomic analyses (including RNA gene expression and DNA methylation profiles, as well as DNA sequencing to identify mutations) on both fresh-frozen and formalin-fixed, paraffin-embedded sections, have identified molecular subtypes of medulloblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_1.3">

14143</a>

1415-<a href="#cit/section_1.6">

14166</a>

1417,<a href="#cit/section_1.19">

141819</a>

1419,<a href="#cit/section_1.20">

142020</a>

1421,<a href="#cit/section_1.47">

142247</a>

1423-<a href="#cit/section_1.54">

142454</a>

1425] These subtypes include those characterized by WNT pathway activation and SHH pathway activation, as well as additional subgroups characterized by <em class="gene-name">

1426MYC</em>

1427 or <em class="gene-name">

1428MYCN</em>

1429 alterations and other genomic alterations.[<a href="#cit/section_1.3">

14303</a>

1431-<a href="#cit/section_1.6">

14326</a>

1433,<a href="#cit/section_1.19">

143419</a>

1435,<a href="#cit/section_1.20">

143620</a>

1437,<a href="#cit/section_1.47">

143847</a>

1439-<a href="#cit/section_1.53">

144053</a>

1441] Children with medulloblastoma whose tumors show WNT pathway activation usually have an excellent prognosis. Within the non-WNT, non-SHH medulloblastoma group, there are subsets of patients with differing prognoses. For example, patients with chromosome 11 loss have an excellent prognosis, similar to the prognosis of those with WNT tumors,[<a href="#cit/section_1.14">

144214</a>

1443,<a href="#cit/section_1.55">

144455</a>

1445,<a href="#cit/section_1.56">

144656</a>

1447] while patients with SHH pathway&#8211;activated tumors have a prognosis that is influenced by the presence or absence of <em class="gene-name">

1448TP53</em>

1449 mutations (favorable vs. unfavorable prognosis, respectively).[<a href="#cit/section_1.56">

145056</a>

1451] The outcome for the remaining patients is less favorable than that for patients with WNT pathway activation. Mutations in medulloblastoma are observed in a subtype-specific manner. <em class="gene-name">

1452CTNNB1</em>

1453 mutations are observed in most WNT-subtype tumors. <em class="gene-name">

1454PTCH1</em>

1455, <em class="gene-name">

1456SMO</em>

1457, and <em class="gene-name">

1458SUFU</em>

1459 mutations are observed in the SHH-subtype tumors. The prognostic significance of recurring mutations is closely aligned with that of the molecular subtype with which they are associated.[<a href="#cit/section_1.4">

14604</a>

1461,<a href="#cit/section_1.57">

146257</a>

1463] At recurrence, the subtype remains unchanged from the original molecular subtype at diagnosis.[<a href="#cit/section_1.58">

146458</a>

1465]</p>

1466<p id="_630" tabindex="-1">

1467For nonmedulloblastoma embryonal tumors, integrative genomic analysis has also identified molecular subtypes with different outcomes. For more detailed information, see the <a href="/types/brain/hp/child-cns-embryonal-treatment-pdq#_1950">

1468Subtypes of nonmedulloblastoma embryonal tumors</a>

1469 section.</p>

1470</section>

1471</section>

1472</section>

1473<section id="_572">

1474<h3 id="_572_toc">

1475Follow-up After Treatment</h3>

1476<p id="_580" tabindex="-1">

1477Relapse in children with embryonal tumors is most likely to occur within the first 18 months of diagnosis.[<a href="#cit/section_1.39">

147839</a>

1479,<a href="#cit/section_1.59">

148059</a>

1481] Surveillance imaging of the brain and spine is usually undertaken at routine intervals during and after treatment (see <a href="/types/brain/hp/child-cns-embryonal-treatment-pdq#_631">

1482Table 2</a>

1483). The frequency of such imaging, designed to detect recurrent disease at an early, asymptomatic state, has been arbitrarily determined and has not been shown to clearly influence survival.[<a href="#cit/section_1.60">

148460</a>

1485-<a href="#cit/section_1.63">

148663</a>

1487] Growth hormone replacement therapy has not been shown to increase the likelihood of disease relapse and should not impact the frequency of surveillance testing.[<a href="#cit/section_1.33">

148833</a>

1489]</p>

1490<table id="_631" class="table-default expandable-container">

1598<p id="_1993" tabindex="-1">

1599Childhood and adolescent cancer survivors require close monitoring because cancer therapy side effects may persist or develop months or years after treatment. For specific information about the incidence, type, and monitoring of late effects in childhood and adolescent cancer survivors, see <a href="/types/childhood-cancers/late-effects-hp-pdq">

1600Late Effects of Treatment for Childhood Cancer</a>

1937<section id="_1876">

1938<h3 id="_1876_toc">

1939Hereditary Cancer Predisposition Syndromes Associated With Medulloblastoma</h3>

1940<p id="_2045" tabindex="-1">

1941Increasingly, subsets of children with brain tumors, including medulloblastoma, have been found to have germline mutations, predisposing them to the development of not only medulloblastoma but also other cancers.[<a href="#cit/section_2.1">

19421</a>

1943,<a href="#cit/section_2.2">

19442</a>

1945] These mutations have obvious connotations for the affected child, siblings, parents and, potentially, other family members in regard to cancer surveillance, prevention, diagnosis, and management. The mutations may also affect specific tumor treatment.</p>

1946<p id="_1877" tabindex="-1">

1947Medulloblastoma can arise in the setting of hereditary cancer predisposition syndromes in approximately 5% of patients.[<a href="#cit/section_2.1">

19481</a>

1949,<a href="#cit/section_2.2">

19502</a>

1951] A large study of over 1,000 patients demonstrated germline mutations in approximately 5% of all patients diagnosed with medulloblastoma. Germline mutations were identified in <em class="gene-name">

1952APC</em>

1953, <em class="gene-name">

1954BRCA2</em>

1955, <em class="gene-name">

1956PALB2</em>

1957, <em class="gene-name">

1958PTCH1</em>

1959, <em class="gene-name">

1960SUFU</em>

1961, and <em class="gene-name">

1962TP53</em>

1963.[<a href="#cit/section_2.2">

19642</a>

1965]</p>

1966<p id="_1878" tabindex="-1">

1967 Syndromes known to be associated with medulloblastoma include the following:</p>

1968<div class="pdq-content-list">

1969<ul id="_1879">

1970<li>

1971Turcot syndrome (related to germline mutations in <em class="gene-name">

1972APC</em>

1973),[<a href="#cit/section_2.3">

19743</a>

1975] exclusive to the WNT-activated subtype.[<a href="#cit/section_2.2">

19762</a>

1977]</li>

1978<li>

1979Rubinstein-Taybi syndrome (related to germline mutations in <em class="gene-name">

1980CREBBP</em>

1981).[<a href="#cit/section_2.4">

19824</a>

1983-<a href="#cit/section_2.6">

19846</a>

1985]</li>

1986<li>

1987Gorlin syndrome (also known as basal cell nevus syndrome or nevoid basal cell carcinoma syndrome, associated with germline <em class="gene-name">

1988PTCH1</em>

1989 and <em class="gene-name">

1990SUFU</em>

1991 mutations).[<a href="#cit/section_2.7">

19927</a>

1993-<a href="#cit/section_2.11">

199411</a>

1995] The risk of developing medulloblastoma in patients with Gorlin syndrome appears to be higher in those with <em class="gene-name">

1996SUFU</em>

1997 mutations than in those with <em class="gene-name">

1998PTCH1</em>

1999 germline mutations. In one study, 2 of 115 individuals (1.7%) with Gorlin syndrome and a <em class="gene-name">

2000PTCH1</em>

2001 mutation developed a pathology-proven medulloblastoma, compared with 3 of 9 individuals (33%) from three families with <em class="gene-name">

2002SUFU</em>

2003-related Gorlin syndrome. Each of the <em class="gene-name">

2004SUFU</em>

2005-related patients developed medulloblastoma before age 3 years.[<a href="#cit/section_2.11">

200611</a>

2007]</li>

2008<li>

2009Li-Fraumeni syndrome (related to germline mutations in <em class="gene-name">

2010TP53</em>

2011).[<a href="#cit/section_2.12">

201212</a>

2013,<a href="#cit/section_2.13">

201413</a>

2015] In one analysis, all germline <em class="gene-name">

2016TP53</em>

2017 mutations were restricted to the sonic hedgehog (SHH)&#8211;activated subtype.[<a href="#cit/section_2.2">

20182</a>

2019]</li>

2020<li>

2021Fanconi anemia (related to <em class="gene-name">

2022BRCA2</em>

2023 mutations).[<a href="#cit/section_2.14">

202414</a>

2025-<a href="#cit/section_2.17">

202617</a>

2027]</li>

2028</ul>

2029</div>

2030<p id="_2046" tabindex="-1">

2031Heterozygous deleterious germline mutations in <em class="gene-name">

2032GPR161</em>

2033 were identified in approximately 3% of cases of SHH medulloblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_2.18">

203418</a>

2035] <em class="gene-name">

2036GPR161</em>

2037 is an inhibitor of SHH signaling. The median age at diagnosis for patients with <em class="gene-name">

2038GPR161</em>

2039 mutations was 1.5 years. Loss of heterozygosity (LOH) at the GPR161 locus was noted in all tumors, with tumors from five of six patients showing copy-neutral LOH of chromosome 1q (on which <em class="gene-name">

2040GPR161</em>

2041 resides). The risk of nonmedulloblastoma cancers in patients with deleterious <em class="gene-name">

2042GPR161</em>

2043 mutations is not defined.</p>

2044<p id="_2092" tabindex="-1">

2045Novel germline loss-of-function variants in the largest subunit of the evolutionarily conserved Elongator complex, <em class="gene-name">

2046ELP1</em>

2047, were identified in 14% of pediatric patients with SHH medulloblastoma. <em class="gene-name">

2048ELP1</em>

2049 was the most common medulloblastoma predisposition gene, and it increased the prevalence of genetic predisposition to 40% among pediatric patients with SHH medulloblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_2.19">

205019</a>

2051]</p>

2052<p id="_1880" tabindex="-1">

2053Sometimes medulloblastoma may be the initial manifestation of the presence of germline mutations in these predisposition genes. Germline testing should be considered in the following circumstances:</p>

2054<div class="pdq-content-list">

2055<ul id="_1881">

2056<li>

2057<em class="gene-name">

2058APC</em>

2059 mutation testing in patients with WNT-activated medulloblastoma in the absence of a somatic <em class="gene-name">

2060CTNNB1</em>

2061 mutation.</li>

2062<li>

2063<em class="gene-name">

2064SUFU</em>

2065, <em class="gene-name">

2066PTCH1</em>

2067, <em class="gene-name">

2068TP53</em>

2069, <em class="gene-name">

2070PALB2</em>

2071, and <em class="gene-name">

2072BRCA2</em>

2073 mutation testing in patients with SHH-activated medulloblastoma. Patients with desmoplastic tumors with extensive nodularity should be carefully evaluated for stigmata of Gorlin syndrome.[<a href="#cit/section_2.7">

20747</a>

2075] One report observed that medulloblastoma with extensive nodularity (MBEN) was associated with Gorlin syndrome in 5 of 12 cases.[<a href="#cit/section_2.7">

20767</a>

2077] Gorlin syndrome is an autosomal dominant disorder in which those affected are predisposed to develop basal cell carcinomas later in life, especially in skin in the radiation portal. The syndrome can be diagnosed early in life by detection of characteristic dermatological and skeletal features such as keratocysts of the jaw, bifid or fused ribs, macrocephaly, and calcifications of the falx.[<a href="#cit/section_2.7">

20787</a>

2079]</li>

2080<li>

2081<em class="gene-name">

2082PALB2</em>

2083 and <em class="gene-name">

2084BRCA2</em>

2085 mutation testing in patients with a family history of <em class="gene-name">

2086BRCA</em>

2087-associated cancers or homologous recombination repair deficiency.</li>

2088</ul>

2089</div>

2090</section>

2091<section id="_1868">

2092<h3 id="_1868_toc">

2093Clinical Presentation</h3>

2094<p id="_1869" tabindex="-1">

2095By definition, medulloblastomas must arise in the posterior fossa.[<a href="#cit/section_2.20">

209620</a>

2097,<a href="#cit/section_2.21">

209821</a>

2099] In approximately 80% of children, medulloblastomas arise in the region of the fourth ventricle. Most of the early symptomatology is related to blockage of cerebrospinal fluid (CSF) and resultant accumulation of CSF in the brain, termed hydrocephalus. Children with medulloblastoma are usually diagnosed within 2 to 3 months of the onset of symptoms. Medulloblastoma commonly presents with the following signs and symptoms:[<a href="#cit/section_2.22">

210022</a>

2101]</p>

2102<div class="pdq-content-list">

2103<ul id="_1870">

2104<li>

2105Relatively abrupt onset of headaches, especially in the morning on waking.</li>

2106<li>

2107Nausea and/or vomiting.</li>

2108<li>

2109Lethargy.</li>

2110<li>

2111Ataxia, including truncal unsteadiness.</li>

2112<li>

2113Some degree of nystagmus.</li>

2114<li>

2115Papilledema.</li>

2116</ul>

2117</div>

2118<p id="_1871" tabindex="-1">

2119Twenty percent of patients with medulloblastoma will not have hydrocephalus at the time of diagnosis and are more likely to present initially with cerebellar deficits. For example, more laterally positioned medulloblastomas of the cerebellum may not result in hydrocephalus and, because of their location, are more likely to result in lateralizing cerebellar dysfunction (appendicular ataxia) manifested by unilateral dysmetria, unsteadiness, and weakness of the sixth and seventh nerves on the same side as the tumor. Later, as the tumor grows toward the midline and blocks CSF, the more classical symptoms associated with hydrocephalus become evident.</p>

2120<p id="_1872" tabindex="-1">

2121Cranial nerve findings are less common, except for unilateral or bilateral sixth nerve palsies, which are usually related to hydrocephalus.[<a href="#cit/section_2.22">

212222</a>

2123] At times, medulloblastomas will present explosively, with the acute onset of lethargy and unconsciousness resulting from hemorrhage within the tumor.</p>

2124<p id="_1873" tabindex="-1">

2125In infants, the presentation of medulloblastoma is more variable and may include the following:</p>

2126<div class="pdq-content-list">

2127<ul id="_1874">

2128<li>

2129Nonspecific lethargy.</li>

2130<li>

2131Psychomotor delays.</li>

2132<li>

2133Loss of developmental milestones.</li>

2134<li>

2135Feeding difficulties.</li>

2136<li>

2137Increase in head circumference.</li>

2138</ul>

2139</div>

2140<p id="_1875" tabindex="-1">

2141On examination, there may be bulging of the anterior fontanel due to increased intracranial pressure and abnormal eye movements, including eyes that are deviated downward (the so-called sun setting sign) because of loss of upgaze secondary to compression of the tectum of the midbrain.</p>

2142</section>

2143<section id="_1891">

2144<h3 id="_1891_toc">

2145Cellular and Molecular Classification</h3>

2146<p id="_2106" tabindex="-1">

2147In the 2021 World Health Organization (WHO) classification, medulloblastoma is classified on the basis of both histological and molecular features. The tumor is classified as medulloblastoma, histologically defined if no molecular testing has been performed.[<a href="#cit/section_2.21">

214821</a>

2149,<a href="#cit/section_2.23">

215023</a>

2151]</p>

2152<div class="pdq-content-list">

2153<ul id="_2107">

2154<li>

2155Medulloblastoma.<div class="pdq-content-list">

2156<ul id="_2108">

2157<li>

2158Medulloblastoma, molecularly defined.<div class="pdq-content-list">

2159<ul id="_2109">

2160<li>

2161Medulloblastoma, WNT-activated.</li>

2162<li>

2163Medulloblastoma, SHH-activated and <em class="gene-name">

2164TP53</em>

2165-wild type.</li>

2166<li>

2167Medulloblastoma, SHH-activated and <em class="gene-name">

2168TP53</em>

2169-mutant.</li>

2170<li>

2171Medulloblastoma, non-WNT/non-SHH.</li>

2172</ul>

2173</div>

2174</li>

2175<li>

2176Medulloblastoma, histologically defined.<div class="pdq-content-list">

2177<ul id="_2122">

2178<li>

2179Desmoplastic nodular medulloblastoma.</li>

2180<li>

2181Medulloblastoma with extensive nodularity.</li>

2182<li>

2183Large cell medulloblastoma.</li>

2184<li>

2185Anaplastic medulloblastoma.</li>

2186</ul>

2187</div>

2188</li>

2189</ul>

2190</div>

2191</li>

2192</ul>

2193</div>

2194<p id="_1894" tabindex="-1">

2195Significant attention has been focused on medulloblastomas that display anaplastic features, including increased nuclear size, marked cytological pleomorphism, numerous mitoses, and apoptotic bodies.[<a href="#cit/section_2.24">

219624</a>

2197,<a href="#cit/section_2.25">

219825</a>

2199] Using the criteria of anaplasia is subjective because most medulloblastomas have some degree of anaplasia. Foci of anaplasia may appear in tumors with histological features of both classic and large cell medulloblastomas, and there is significant overlap between the anaplastic and large cell variants, which are frequently termed large cell/anaplastic medulloblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_2.24">

220024</a>

2201,<a href="#cit/section_2.25">

220225</a>

2203] One convention is to consider medulloblastomas as anaplastic when anaplasia is diffuse (variably defined as anaplasia occurring in 50% to 80% of the tumor).</p>

2204<p id="_1895" tabindex="-1">

2205The incidence of medulloblastoma with the desmoplastic/nodular histological variant, which most commonly arises in a cerebellar hemisphere, is higher in infants, is less common in children, and increases again in adolescents and adults. The desmoplastic/nodular histological variant is different from MBEN; the nodular variant has an expanded lobular architecture. The MBEN subtype occurs almost exclusively in infants and generally carries an excellent prognosis.[<a href="#cit/section_2.7">

22067</a>

2207,<a href="#cit/section_2.26">

220826</a>

2209,<a href="#cit/section_2.27">

221027</a>

2211] However, a recent report used transcriptome sequencing to identify a subset of patients with MBENs that had a high frequency of germline alterations in <em class="gene-name">

2212PTCH1</em>

2213 or <em class="gene-name">

2214SUFU</em>

2215. These patients had less favorable outcomes.[<a href="#cit/section_2.28">

221628</a>

2217]</p>

2218<section id="_1896">

2219<section id="_sm_CDR0000779394_1">

2220<h5 id="_sm_CDR0000779394_1_toc">

2221Molecular subtypes of medulloblastoma</h5>

2222<p id="_sm_CDR0000779394_460" tabindex="-1">

2223Multiple medulloblastoma subtypes have been identified by integrative molecular analysis.[<a href="#cit/section_2.29">

222429</a>

2225-<a href="#cit/section_2.52">

222652</a>

2227] Since 2012, the general consensus is that medulloblastoma can be molecularly separated into at least four core subtypes, including WNT-activated, sonic hedgehog (SHH)&#8211;activated, group 3, and group 4. In the 2021 World Health Organization (WHO) classification, the SHH subgroup has been divided into two groups on the basis of <em class="gene-name">

2228TP53</em>

2229 status. Group 3 and group 4, which require methylation analysis for reliable separation, have been combined into medulloblastoma, non-WNT/non-SHH. Because the group 3 and group 4 terminology has been used extensively in completed studies and is still in use in ongoing and planned studies, this nomenclature will be maintained throughout the clinical discussion in this summary.[<a href="#cit/section_2.21">

223021</a>

2231,<a href="#cit/section_2.23">

223223</a>

2233]</p>

2234<p id="_sm_CDR0000779394_778" tabindex="-1">

2235Different regions of the same tumor are likely to have other disparate genetic mutations, adding to the complexity of devising effective molecularly targeted therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_2.47">

223647</a>

2237] However, the major subtypes noted above remain stable across primary and metastatic components.[<a href="#cit/section_2.48">

223848</a>

2239,<a href="#cit/section_2.51">

224051</a>

2241]</p>

2242<p id="_sm_CDR0000779394_763" tabindex="-1">

2243Further subclassification within these subgroups is possible, which will provide even more prognostic information.[<a href="#cit/section_2.49">

224449</a>

2245-<a href="#cit/section_2.51">

224651</a>

2247]</p>

2248<section id="_sm_CDR0000779394_742">

2249<h6 id="_sm_CDR0000779394_742_toc">

2250Medulloblastoma, WNT-activated</h6>

2251<p id="_sm_CDR0000779394_743" tabindex="-1">

2252 WNT tumors are medulloblastomas with aberrations in the WNT signaling pathway and represent approximately 10% of all medulloblastomas.[<a href="#cit/section_2.49">

225349</a>

2254] WNT medulloblastomas show a WNT signaling gene expression signature and beta-catenin nuclear staining by immunohistochemistry.[<a href="#cit/section_2.53">

225553</a>

2256] They are usually histologically classified as <em>

2257classic medulloblastoma</em>

2258 tumors and rarely have a large cell/anaplastic appearance. WNT medulloblastomas generally occur in older patients (median age, 10 years) and are infrequently metastasized at diagnosis. Recent studies have demonstrated the value of methylation profiling in identifying WNT-activated medulloblastomas. These studies included cases that would not be detected using other current testing methods (e.g., beta-catenin immunohistochemistry, <em class="gene-name">

2259CTNNB1</em>

2260 mutation analysis, and evaluation for monosomy 6).[<a href="#cit/section_2.54">

226154</a>

2262]</p>

2263<p id="_sm_CDR0000779394_744" tabindex="-1">

2264<em class="gene-name">

2265CTNNB1</em>

2266 mutations are observed in 85% to 90% of WNT medulloblastoma cases, with <em class="gene-name">

2267APC</em>

2268 mutations detected in many of the cases that lack <em class="gene-name">

2269CTNNB1</em>

2270 mutations. Patients with WNT medulloblastoma whose tumors have <em class="gene-name">

2271APC</em>

2272 mutations often have Turcot syndrome (i.e., germline <em class="gene-name">

2273APC</em>

2274 mutations).[<a href="#cit/section_2.50">

227550</a>

2276] In addition to <em class="gene-name">

2277CTNNB1</em>

2278 mutations, WNT medulloblastoma tumors show 6q loss (monosomy 6) in 80% to 90% of cases. While monosomy 6 is observed in most medulloblastoma patients younger than 18 years at diagnosis, it appears to be much less common (approximately 25% of cases) in patients older than 18 years.[<a href="#cit/section_2.49">

227949</a>

2280,<a href="#cit/section_2.53">

228153</a>

2282]</p>

2283<p id="_sm_CDR0000779394_745" tabindex="-1">

2284The WNT subset is primarily observed in older children, adolescents, and adults and does not show a male predominance. The subset is believed to have brain stem origin, from the embryonal rhombic lip region.[<a href="#cit/section_2.55">

228555</a>

2286] WNT medulloblastomas are associated with a very good outcome in children, especially in individuals whose tumors have beta-catenin nuclear staining and proven 6q loss and/or <em class="gene-name">

2287CTNNB1</em>

2288 mutations.[<a href="#cit/section_2.44">

228944</a>

2290,<a href="#cit/section_2.56">

229156</a>

2292-<a href="#cit/section_2.58">

229358</a>

2294] Retrospective studies have suggested that additional <em class="gene-name">

2295TP53</em>

2296 mutations and <em class="gene-name">

2297OTX2</em>

2298 copy number gains may be associated with a worse prognosis for patients with WNT medulloblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_2.59">

229959</a>

2300]</p>

2301</section>

2302<section id="_sm_CDR0000779394_746">

2303<h6 id="_sm_CDR0000779394_746_toc">

2304Medulloblastoma, SHH-activated and <em class="gene-name">

2305TP53</em>

2306-mutant and medulloblastoma, SHH-activated and <em class="gene-name">

2307TP53</em>

2308-wild type </h6>

2309<p id="_sm_CDR0000779394_747" tabindex="-1">

2310 SHH tumors are medulloblastomas with aberrations in the SHH pathway and represent approximately 25% of medulloblastoma cases.[<a href="#cit/section_2.49">

231149</a>

2312] SHH medulloblastomas are characterized by chromosome 9q deletions; desmoplastic/nodular histology; and mutations in SHH pathway genes, including <em class="gene-name">

2313PTCH1</em>

2314, <em class="gene-name">

2315PTCH2</em>

2316, <em class="gene-name">

2317SMO</em>

2318, <em class="gene-name">

2319SUFU</em>

2320, and <em class="gene-name">

2321GLI2</em>

2322.[<a href="#cit/section_2.53">

232353</a>

2324]</p>

2325<p id="_sm_CDR0000779394_767" tabindex="-1">

2326Heterozygous deleterious germline mutations in the <em class="gene-name">

2327GPR161</em>

2328 gene were identified in approximately 3% of cases of SHH medulloblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_2.18">

232918</a>

2330] <em class="gene-name">

2331GPR161</em>

2332 is an inhibitor of SHH signaling. Median age at diagnosis for <em class="gene-name">

2333GPR161</em>

2334-mutated cases was 1.5 years. Loss of heterozygosity (LOH) at the GPR161 locus was noted in all tumors, with tumors from five of six patients showing copy-neutral LOH of chromosome 1q (on which <em class="gene-name">

2335GPR161</em>

2336 resides).</p>

2337<p id="_sm_CDR0000779394_768" tabindex="-1">

2338Mutations in the third nucleotide (r.3A&gt;G) of the U1 spliceosomal small nuclear RNAs (snRNAs) are highly specific for SHH medulloblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_2.60">

233960</a>

2340,<a href="#cit/section_2.61">

234161</a>

2342] U1 snRNA r.3A&gt;G mutations are observed in virtually all cases of SHH medulloblastoma in adults, in approximately one-third of cases in children and adolescents, and are absent in infant cases.[<a href="#cit/section_2.61">

234361</a>

2344] U1 snRNA mutations disrupt RNA splicing, leading to inactivation of tumor-suppressor genes (e.g., <em class="gene-name">

2345PTCH1</em>

2346) and activation of oncogenes (e.g., <em class="gene-name">

2347GLI2</em>

2348). The significance of U1 snRNA r.3A&gt;G mutations in specific SHH medulloblastoma subtypes is described below.</p>

2349<p id="_sm_CDR0000779394_748" tabindex="-1">

2350SHH medulloblastomas show a bimodal age distribution and are observed primarily in children younger than 3 years and in older adolescence/adulthood. The tumors are believed to emanate from the external granular layer of the cerebellum. The heterogeneity in age at presentation maps to distinctive subsets identified by further molecular characterization, as follows:</p>

2351<div class="pdq-content-list">

2352<ul id="_sm_CDR0000779394_749">

2353<li>

2354The subset of medulloblastoma most common in <strong>

2355children aged 3 to 16 years</strong>

2356, termed SHH-alpha, is enriched for <em class="gene-name">

2357MYCN</em>

2358 and <em class="gene-name">

2359GLI2</em>

2360 amplifications, with <em class="gene-name">

2361TP53</em>

2362 mutations commonly co-occurring with one of these amplifications.[<a href="#cit/section_2.49">

236349</a>

2364,<a href="#cit/section_2.51">

236551</a>

2366] <em class="gene-name">

2367PTCH1</em>

2368 mutations occur in this subtype and are mutually exclusive with <em class="gene-name">

2369TP53</em>

2370 mutations (often germline), while <em class="gene-name">

2371SMO</em>

2372 and <em class="gene-name">

2373SUFU</em>

2374 mutations are rare.[<a href="#cit/section_2.51">

237551</a>

2376,<a href="#cit/section_2.62">

237762</a>

2378] U1 snRNA mutations occur in approximately 25% of SHH-alpha medulloblastoma cases and are associated with a very poor prognosis.[<a href="#cit/section_2.61">

237961</a>

2380]</li>

2381<li>

2382Two SHH subtypes that occur primarily in <strong>

2383children younger than 3 years</strong>

2384 have been described.[<a href="#cit/section_2.49">

238549</a>

2386] One of these subtypes, termed SHH-beta, is more frequently metastatic, with more frequent focal amplifications.[<a href="#cit/section_2.63">

238763</a>

2388] The second of these subtypes, termed SHH-gamma, is enriched for the medulloblastoma with extensive nodularity (MBEN) histology. SHH pathway mutations in children younger than 3 years with medulloblastoma include <em class="gene-name">

2389PTCH1</em>

2390 and <em class="gene-name">

2391SUFU</em>

2392 mutations.[<a href="#cit/section_2.51">

239351</a>

2394] <em class="gene-name">

2395SUFU</em>

2396 mutations are rarely observed in older children and adults, and they are commonly germline events.[<a href="#cit/section_2.62">

239762</a>

2398]<p id="_sm_CDR0000779394_738" tabindex="-1">

2399Reports that used DNA methylation arrays have also identified two subtypes of SHH medulloblastoma in young children.[<a href="#cit/section_2.27">

240027</a>

2401,<a href="#cit/section_2.63">

240263</a>

2403] One of the subtypes contained all of the cases with <em class="gene-name">

2404SMO</em>

2405 mutations, and it was associated with a favorable prognosis. The other subtype had most of the <em class="gene-name">

2406SUFU</em>

2407 mutations, and it was associated with a much lower progression-free survival (PFS) rate. <em class="gene-name">

2408PTCH1</em>

2409 mutations were present in both subtypes.</p>

2410</li>

2411<li>

2412A fourth SHH subtype, termed SHH-delta, includes most of the <strong>

2413adult</strong>

2414 cases of SHH medulloblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_2.49">

241549</a>

2416] Virtually all cases of SHH-delta medulloblastoma have the U1 snRNA r.A&gt;3 mutation,[<a href="#cit/section_2.61">

241761</a>

2418] and approximately 90% of cases have <em class="gene-name">

2419TERT</em>

2420 promoter mutations.[<a href="#cit/section_2.49">

242149</a>

2422] <em class="gene-name">

2423PTCH1</em>

2424 and <em class="gene-name">

2425SMO</em>

2426 mutations are also observed in adults with SHH medulloblastoma.</li>

2427</ul>

2428</div>

2429<p id="_sm_CDR0000779394_750" tabindex="-1">

2430The outcome for patients with nonmetastatic SHH medulloblastoma is relatively favorable for children younger than 3 years and for adults.[<a href="#cit/section_2.49">

243149</a>

2432] Young children with the MBEN histology have a particularly favorable prognosis.[<a href="#cit/section_2.7">

24337</a>

2434,<a href="#cit/section_2.26">

243526</a>

2436,<a href="#cit/section_2.64">

243764</a>

2438-<a href="#cit/section_2.66">

243966</a>

2440] Patients with SHH medulloblastoma at greatest risk of treatment failure are children older than 3 years whose tumors have <em class="gene-name">

2441TP53</em>

2442 mutations, often with co-occurring <em class="gene-name">

2443GLI2</em>

2444 or <em class="gene-name">

2445MYCN</em>

2446 amplification and large cell/anaplastic histology.[<a href="#cit/section_2.49">

244749</a>

2448,<a href="#cit/section_2.62">

244962</a>

2450,<a href="#cit/section_2.67">

245167</a>

2452]</p>

2453<p id="_sm_CDR0000779394_751" tabindex="-1">

2454Patients with unfavorable molecular findings have an unfavorable prognosis, with fewer than 50% of patients surviving after conventional treatment.[<a href="#cit/section_2.45">

245545</a>

2456,<a href="#cit/section_2.62">

245762</a>

2458,<a href="#cit/section_2.67">

245967</a>

2460-<a href="#cit/section_2.70">

246170</a>

2462]</p>

2463<p id="_sm_CDR0000779394_752" tabindex="-1">

2464The 2021 WHO classification identifies SHH medulloblastoma with a <em class="gene-name">

2465TP53</em>

2466 mutation as a distinctive entity (medulloblastoma, SHH-activated and <em class="gene-name">

2467TP53</em>

2468-mutant).[<a href="#cit/section_2.21">

246921</a>

2470,<a href="#cit/section_2.23">

247123</a>

2472] Approximately 25% of SHH-activated medulloblastoma cases have <em class="gene-name">

2473TP53</em>

2474 mutations, with a high percentage of these cases also showing a <em class="gene-name">

2475TP53</em>

2476 germline mutation (9 of 20 in one study). These patients are commonly between the ages of 5 years and 18 years and have a worse outcome (overall survival at 5 years, &lt;50%).[<a href="#cit/section_2.69">

247769</a>

2478] The tumors often show large cell anaplastic histology.[<a href="#cit/section_2.69">

247969</a>

2480]</p>

2481</section>

2482<section id="_sm_CDR0000779394_753">

2483<h6 id="_sm_CDR0000779394_753_toc">

2484Medulloblastoma, non&#8211;WNT/non&#8211;SHH-activated</h6>

2485<p id="_sm_CDR0000779394_754" tabindex="-1">

2486 The WHO classification combines group 3 and group 4 medulloblastoma cases into a single entity, partly on the basis of the absence of immediate clinical impact for this distinction. Group 3 medulloblastoma represents approximately 25% of medulloblastoma cases, while group 4 medulloblastoma represents approximately 40% of medulloblastoma cases.[<a href="#cit/section_2.49">

248749</a>

2488,<a href="#cit/section_2.53">

248953</a>

2490] Both group 3 and group 4 medulloblastoma patients are predominantly male.[<a href="#cit/section_2.38">

249138</a>

2492,<a href="#cit/section_2.51">

249351</a>

2494] Group 3 and group 4 medulloblastomas can be further subdivided on the basis of characteristics such as gene expression and DNA methylation profiles, but the optimal approach to their subdivision is not established.[<a href="#cit/section_2.49">

249549</a>

2496,<a href="#cit/section_2.50">

249750</a>

2498]</p>

2499<p id="_sm_CDR0000779394_755" tabindex="-1">

2500Various genomic alterations are observed in group 3 and group 4 medulloblastomas; however, no single alteration occurs in more than 10% to 20% of cases. Genomic alterations include the following:</p>

2501<div class="pdq-content-list">

2502<ul id="_sm_CDR0000779394_756">

2503<li>

2504<em class="gene-name">

2505MYC</em>

2506 amplification was the most common distinctive alteration reported for group 3 medulloblastoma, occurring in approximately 15% of cases.[<a href="#cit/section_2.43">

250743</a>

2508,<a href="#cit/section_2.50">

250950</a>

2510]</li>

2511<li>

2512The most common distinctive genomic alteration described for group 4 medulloblastoma (observed in approximately 15% of cases) was activation of <em class="gene-name">

2513PRDM6</em>

2514 by enhancer hijacking, resulting from the tandem duplication of the adjacent <em class="gene-name">

2515SNCAIP</em>

2516 gene.[<a href="#cit/section_2.50">

251750</a>

2518]</li>

2519<li>

2520Other genomic alterations were observed in both group 3 and group 4 cases, including <em class="gene-name">

2521MYCN</em>

2522 amplification and structural variants leading to <em class="gene-name">

2523GFI1</em>

2524 or <em class="gene-name">

2525GFI1B</em>

2526 overexpression through enhancer hijacking.</li>

2527<li>

2528Isochromosome 17q (i17q) is the most common cytogenetic abnormality and is observed in a high percentage of group 4 cases, as well as in group 3 cases, but it is rarely observed in WNT and SHH medulloblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_2.43">

252943</a>

2530,<a href="#cit/section_2.50">

253150</a>

2532] Prognosis for group 3 and group 4 patients does not appear to be affected by the presence of i17q.[<a href="#cit/section_2.71">

253371</a>

2534]</li>

2535</ul>

2536</div>

2537<p id="_sm_CDR0000779394_757" tabindex="-1">

2538Group 3 patients with <em class="gene-name">

2539MYC</em>

2540 amplification or <em class="gene-name">

2541MYC</em>

2542 overexpression have a poor prognosis.[<a href="#cit/section_2.51">

254351</a>

2544] Fewer than 50% of these patients survive 5 years after diagnosis.[<a href="#cit/section_2.49">

254549</a>

2546] This poor prognosis is especially true in children younger than 4 years at diagnosis.[<a href="#cit/section_2.45">

254745</a>

2548] However, patients with group 3 medulloblastoma without <em class="gene-name">

2549MYC</em>

2550 amplification who are older than 3 years have a prognosis similar to that of most patients with non-WNT medulloblastoma, with a 5-year PFS rate higher than 70%.[<a href="#cit/section_2.68">

255168</a>

2552,<a href="#cit/section_2.71">

255371</a>

2554]</p>

2555<p id="_sm_CDR0000779394_758" tabindex="-1">

2556Group 4 medulloblastomas occur throughout infancy and childhood and into adulthood. The prognosis for group 4 medulloblastoma patients is similar to that for patients with other non-WNT medulloblastomas and may be affected by additional factors such as the presence of metastatic disease, chromosome 11q loss, and chromosome 17p loss.[<a href="#cit/section_2.42">

255742</a>

2558,<a href="#cit/section_2.43">

255943</a>

2560,<a href="#cit/section_2.49">

256149</a>

2562,<a href="#cit/section_2.67">

256367</a>

2564] One study found that group 4 patients with either chromosome 11 loss or gain of chromosome 17 were low risk, regardless of metastases. In cases lacking both of these cytogenetic features, metastasis at presentation differentiated between high and intermediate risk.[<a href="#cit/section_2.67">

256567</a>

2566]</p>

2567<p id="_sm_CDR0000779394_764" tabindex="-1">

2568For group 3 and group 4 standard-risk patients (i.e., without <em class="gene-name">

2569MYC</em>

2570 amplification or metastatic disease), the gain or loss of whole chromosomes appears to connote a favorable prognosis. This finding was derived from the data of 91 patients with non-WNT/non-SHH medulloblastoma enrolled in the <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT01351870">

2571SIOP-PNET-4 (NCT01351870)</a>

2572 clinical trial and was confirmed in an independent group of 70 children with non-WNT/non-SHH medulloblastoma treated between 1990 and 2014.[<a href="#cit/section_2.71">

257371</a>

2574] Chromosomal abnormalities include the following:</p>

2575<div class="pdq-content-list">

2576<ul id="_sm_CDR0000779394_762">

2577<li>

2578The gain/loss of one or more whole chromosomes was associated with a 5-year event-free survival (EFS) rate of 93%, compared with 64% for no whole chromosome gains/losses.</li>

2579<li>

2580The most common whole chromosomal gains/losses are gain of chromosome 7 and loss of chromosomes 8 and 11.</li>

2581<li>

2582The optimally performing prognosis discriminator was determined to be the occurrence of two or more of the following aberrations: chromosome 7 gain, chromosome 8 loss, and chromosome 11 loss. Approximately 40% of group 3 and group 4 standard-risk patients had two or more of these chromosomal aberrations and had a 5-year EFS rate of 100%, compared with 68% for patients with fewer than two aberrations.</li>

2583<li>

2584In an independent cohort, the prognostic significance of two or more gains/losses versus zero or one gain/loss of chromosomes 7, 8, and 11 was confirmed (5-year EFS rate, 95% for patients with two or more vs. 59% for patients with one or fewer).</li>

2585</ul>

2586</div>

2587</section>

2588<p id="_sm_CDR0000779394_759" tabindex="-1">

2589The classification of medulloblastoma into the four major subtypes will likely be altered in the future.[<a href="#cit/section_2.49">

259049</a>

2591,<a href="#cit/section_2.50">

259250</a>

2593,<a href="#cit/section_2.70">

259470</a>

2595,<a href="#cit/section_2.72">

259672</a>

2597,<a href="#cit/section_2.73">

259873</a>

2599] Further subdivision within subgroups based on molecular characteristics is likely because each of the subgroups is further molecularly dissected, although the studies are nearing consensus as data from multiple independent studies are merged. As an example, using complementary bioinformatics approaches, concordance was analyzed between multiple large published cohorts, and a more unified subgrouping was described. For children with group 3 and group 4 medulloblastomas, eight distinct subgroups were determined by DNA methylation clustering. Specific subgroups had different prognoses.[<a href="#cit/section_2.42">

260042</a>

2601,<a href="#cit/section_2.53">

260253</a>

2603,<a href="#cit/section_2.62">

260462</a>

2605,<a href="#cit/section_2.74">

260674</a>

2607]</p>

2608<p id="_sm_CDR0000779394_760" tabindex="-1">

2609It is unknown whether the classification for adults with medulloblastoma has a predictive ability similar to that for children.[<a href="#cit/section_2.43">

261043</a>

2611,<a href="#cit/section_2.45">

261245</a>

2613] In one study of adult medulloblastoma, <em class="gene-name">

2614MYC</em>

2615 oncogene amplifications were rarely observed, and tumors with 6q deletion and WNT activation (as identified by nuclear beta-catenin staining) did not share the excellent prognosis seen in pediatric medulloblastomas, although another study did confirm an excellent prognosis for WNT-activated tumors in adults.[<a href="#cit/section_2.43">

261643</a>

2617,<a href="#cit/section_2.45">

261845</a>

2619]</p>

2620</section>

2621</section>

2622</section>

2623<section id="_1882">

2624<h3 id="_1882_toc">

2625Staging Evaluation</h3>

2626<p id="_1883" tabindex="-1">

2627Historically, staging was based on an intraoperative evaluation of both the size and extent of the tumor, coupled with postoperative neuroimaging of the brain and spine and cytological evaluation of CSF (the Chang system). Intraoperative evaluation of the extent of the tumor has been supplanted by neuraxis imaging before diagnosis and postoperative imaging to determine the amount of primary site residual disease. The following tests and procedures are now used for staging: </p>

2628<div class="pdq-content-list">

2629<ul id="_1884">

2630<li>

2631Magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) of the brain and spine (often done preoperatively).</li>

2632<li>

2633Postoperative MRI of the brain to determine the amount of residual disease.</li>

2634<li>

2635Lumbar CSF analysis.[<a href="#cit/section_2.75">

263675</a>

2637-<a href="#cit/section_2.77">

263877</a>

2639] </li>

2640</ul>

2641</div>

2642<p id="_1885" tabindex="-1">

2643The tumor extent is defined as:</p>

2644<div class="pdq-content-list">

2645<ul id="_1886">

2646<li>

2647M<sub>

26480</sub>

2649: No dissemination.</li>

2650<li>

2651M<sub>

26521</sub>

2653: CSF-positive cytology only.</li>

2654<li>

2655M<sub>

26562</sub>

2657: Gross nodular seeding in cerebellar-cerebral subarachnoid space and/or lateral or third ventricle.</li>

2658<li>

2659M<sub>

26603</sub>

2661: Gross nodular seeding in spinal subarachnoid space.</li>

2662<li>

2663M<sub>

26644</sub>

2665: Extraneural metastasis.</li>

2666</ul>

2667</div>

2668<p id="_1887" tabindex="-1">

2669Postoperative degree of residual disease is designated as:</p>

2670<div class="pdq-content-list">

2671<ul id="_1888">

2672<li>

2673Gross-total resection/near-total resection: No or minimal (&#8804;1.5 cm<span class="sup">

26742</span>

2675) evidence of residual disease after resection.</li>

2676<li>

2677Subtotal resection: Residual disease after diagnosis (&gt;1.5 cm<span class="sup">

26782</span>

2679 of measurable residual disease).</li>

2680<li>

2681Biopsy: No tumor resection; only a sample of tumor tissue is removed.</li>

2682</ul>

2683</div>

2684<p id="_1889" tabindex="-1">

2685Since the 1990s, prospective studies have been performed using this staging system to separate patients into average-risk and high-risk medulloblastoma subgroups.[<a href="#cit/section_2.76">

268676</a>

2687-<a href="#cit/section_2.78">

268878</a>

2689]</p>

2690<p id="_1890" tabindex="-1">

2691The presence of diffuse (&gt;50% of the pathological specimen) histological anaplasia has been incorporated as an addition to staging systems. If diffuse anaplasia is found, patients with otherwise average-risk disease are upstaged to high-risk disease.</p>

2692</section>

2693<section id="_1897">

2694<h3 id="_1897_toc">

2695Risk Stratification</h3>

2696<p id="_1898" tabindex="-1">

2697Risk stratification is based on neuroradiographical evaluation for disseminated disease, CSF cytological examination, postoperative neuroimaging evaluation for the amount of residual disease, and patient age. For more information, see the <a href="/types/brain/hp/child-cns-embryonal-treatment-pdq#_1882">

2698Staging Evaluation</a>

2699 section. Patients older than 3 years with medulloblastoma have been stratified into the following two risk groups:</p>

2700<div class="pdq-content-list">

2701<ul id="_1899">

2702<li>

2703<strong>

2704Average risk:</strong>

2705 Children older than 3 years with tumors that are totally resected or near-totally resected (&#8804;1.5 cm<span class="sup">

27062</span>

2707 of residual disease) and who have no metastatic disease.[<a href="#cit/section_2.76">

270876</a>

2709]</li>

2710<li>

2711<strong>

2712High risk:</strong>

2713 Children older than 3 years with metastatic disease and/or subtotal resection (&gt;1.5 cm<span class="sup">

27142</span>

2715 of residual disease).[<a href="#cit/section_2.76">

271676</a>

2717] Metastatic disease includes neuroradiographical evidence of disseminated disease, positive cytology in lumbar or ventricular CSF obtained more than 14 days after surgery, or extraneural disease.[<a href="#cit/section_2.76">

271876</a>

2719] Children with tumors showing diffuse anaplasia and who otherwise would be considered average risk are assigned to the high-risk group.[<a href="#cit/section_2.25">

272025</a>

2721,<a href="#cit/section_2.37">

272237</a>

2723]</li>

2724</ul>

2725</div>

2726<p id="_1900" tabindex="-1">

2727For younger children (younger than 3 years in some studies and younger than 4 or 5 years in others), similar separation into average-risk (no dissemination and &#8804;1.5 cm<span class="sup">

27282</span>

2729 of residual disease) or high-risk (disseminated disease and/or &gt;1.5 cm<span class="sup">

27302</span>

2731 of residual disease) groups has been used. Histological findings of desmoplasia have also been used to connote a more favorable risk subgrouping, especially for the MBEN subgroup.[<a href="#cit/section_2.79">

273279</a>

2733,<a href="#cit/section_2.80">

273480</a>

2735]</p>

2736<p id="_1901" tabindex="-1">

2737 Assigning a risk group on the basis of the extent of resection and disease at diagnosis may not predict treatment outcome. Molecular genetics and histological factors may be more informative, although they must be evaluated in the context of patient age, the extent of disease at the time of diagnosis, and treatment received.[<a href="#cit/section_2.42">

273842</a>

2739,<a href="#cit/section_2.70">

274070</a>

2741,<a href="#cit/section_2.81">

274281</a>

2743] The risk characterizations of molecular subdivisions are changing and are becoming integrated into risk stratification schema to assign treatment in North American prospective studies (e.g., <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT01878617">

2744NCT01878617</a>

2745 and <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT02724579">

2746NCT02724579</a>

2747).[<a href="#cit/section_2.72">

274872</a>

2749]</p>

2750</section>
