2847<section id="_1903">

2848<h4 id="_1903_toc">

2849Surgery</h4>

2850<p id="_1904" tabindex="-1">

2851Surgery is considered a standard part of treatment for histological confirmation of tumor type and as a means to improve outcome. Total or near-total resections are considered optimal if they can be performed safely.[<a href="#cit/section_2.82">

285282</a>

2853,<a href="#cit/section_2.83">

285483</a>

2855]</p>

2856<p id="_1905" tabindex="-1">

2857Postoperatively, children may have significant neurological deficits caused by preoperative tumor-related brain injury, hydrocephalus, or surgery-related brain injury.[<a href="#cit/section_2.84">

285884</a>

2859][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2860Level of evidence C1</a>

2861] A significant number of patients with medulloblastoma develop cerebellar mutism syndrome (also known as posterior fossa syndrome). Symptoms of cerebellar mutism syndrome include the following:</p>

2862<div class="pdq-content-list">

2863<ul id="_1906">

2864<li>

2865Delayed onset of speech.</li>

2866<li>

2867Suprabulbar palsies.</li>

2868<li>

2869Ataxia.</li>

2870<li>

2871Hypotonia.</li>

2872<li>

2873Emotional lability.</li>

2874</ul>

2875</div>

2876<p id="_1907" tabindex="-1">

2877The etiology of cerebellar mutism syndrome (also known as posterior fossa syndrome) remains unclear, although cerebellar vermian damage and/or disruption of cerebellar-cortical tracts has been postulated as the possible cause of the mutism.[<a href="#cit/section_2.85">

287885</a>

2879,<a href="#cit/section_2.86">

288086</a>

2881]; [<a href="#cit/section_2.87">

288287</a>

2883][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2884Level of evidence C1</a>

2885] In two Children&#8217;s Cancer Group studies that evaluated children with both average-risk and high-risk medulloblastoma, the syndrome was identified in nearly 25% of patients.[<a href="#cit/section_2.86">

288686</a>

2887-<a href="#cit/section_2.88">

288888</a>

2889]; [<a href="#cit/section_2.89">

289089</a>

2891][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2892Level of evidence C1</a>

2893] A retrospective analysis of 370 patients with medulloblastoma identified younger age, larger tumor size, and midline tumor location as risk factors for developing mutism. This finding is consistent with previous observations. Investigators also observed a correlation between medulloblastoma subtype and risk of mutism. Mutism was more common in patients with group 3 and group 4 medulloblastomas (30%&#8211;35% of patients) and less frequent in children with SHH-activated tumors (7% of patients).[<a href="#cit/section_2.90">

289490</a>

2895] A prospective study that included longitudinal neurological examination of 178 patients with medulloblastoma identified similar risk factors for mutism (higher risk with younger age; lower risk with SHH-subtype), most likely because SHH-activated tumors tend to be located in the hemispheres and not in the midline. The study also reported a higher risk of developing mutism in patients who undergo tumor resections at low-volume surgery centers.[<a href="#cit/section_2.91">

289691</a>

2897] Approximately 50% of patients with this syndrome manifest long-term, permanent neurological and neurocognitive sequelae.[<a href="#cit/section_2.87">

289887</a>

2899,<a href="#cit/section_2.89">

290089</a>

2901]</p>

2902</section>

2903<section id="_1908">

2904<h4 id="_1908_toc">

2905Radiation therapy</h4>

2906<p id="_1909" tabindex="-1">

2907Radiation therapy to the primary tumor site is usually in the range of 54 Gy to 55.8 Gy.[<a href="#cit/section_2.92">

290892</a>

2909] In most instances, this is given with a margin of 1 cm to 2 cm around the primary tumor site, preferably by conformal techniques.[<a href="#cit/section_2.92">

291092</a>

2911] Reducing boost volumes for the whole posterior fossa and to the tumor bed plus margins did not compromise outcomes in average-risk patients in the Children's Oncology Group (COG) <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00085735">

2912ACNS0331 (NCT00085735)</a>

2913 study.[<a href="#cit/section_2.58">

291458</a>

2915][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2916Level of evidence A1</a>

2917] For all medulloblastomas in children older than 3 or 4 years at diagnosis, craniospinal radiation therapy is given at doses ranging between 23.4 Gy and 36 Gy, depending on risk factors such as extent of disease at diagnosis. A prospective phase II toxicity study of proton radiation therapy [<a href="#cit/section_2.93">

291893</a>

2919] and a retrospective efficacy report of protons versus photons for medulloblastoma [<a href="#cit/section_2.94">

292094</a>

2921] demonstrated equivalent outcomes for progression-free survival (PFS), overall survival (OS), patterns of relapse, and delayed toxic effects. A retrospective study of 84 children who received either proton (n = 38) or photon (n = 46) radiation therapy demonstrated similar rates of grade 3 and grade 4 ototoxicity despite low mean cochlear doses in children who received proton radiation therapy, suggesting that other factors (e.g. cisplatin, initial tumor location in relationship to the vestibulocochlear nerve [eighth cranial nerve]) contribute to ototoxicity.[<a href="#cit/section_2.95">

292295</a>

2923] The comparative outcomes of these treatment technologies are under investigation.</p>

2924<p id="_1910" tabindex="-1">

2925Chemotherapy is usually administered during and after radiation therapy.</p>

2926<p id="_1911" tabindex="-1">

2927For children younger than 3 years, efforts are made to omit or delay radiation therapy, given the profound impact of radiation at this age. Children of all ages are susceptible to the adverse effects of radiation on brain development. Debilitating effects on neurocognitive development, growth, and endocrine function have been frequently observed, especially in younger children.[<a href="#cit/section_2.96">

292896</a>

2929-<a href="#cit/section_2.100">

2930100</a>

2931]</p>

2932</section>

2933<section id="_1912">

2934<h4 id="_1912_toc">

2935Chemotherapy</h4>

2936<p id="_1913" tabindex="-1">

2937Chemotherapy, usually given during and after radiation therapy, is a standard component of treatment for older children with medulloblastoma and other embryonal tumors. Chemotherapy can be used to delay and sometimes obviate the need for radiation therapy in 20% to 40% of children younger than 3 to 4 years with nondisseminated medulloblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_2.101">

2938101</a>

2939,<a href="#cit/section_2.102">

2940102</a>

2941]; [<a href="#cit/section_2.100">

2942100</a>

2943][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2944Level of evidence C1</a>

2945]</p>

2946</section>

2947</section>

2948<section id="_1914">

2949<h3 id="_1914_toc">

2950Treatment of Childhood Medulloblastoma</h3>

2951<section id="_1916">

2952<h4 id="_1916_toc">

2953Treatment of younger children with medulloblastoma</h4>

2954<p id="_1918" tabindex="-1">

2955The 5-year event-free survival (EFS) rates for young children with medulloblastoma, arbitrarily described in the past as aged 3 years and younger at diagnosis, have ranged between 30% and 70%. There is no consensus as to what age constitutes a younger population of children with medulloblastoma who are best treated with immediate postsurgery chemotherapy and delayed or no radiation therapy. Most studies agree that in patients aged 3 years and younger, initial chemotherapy should be strongly considered. In patients between the ages of 3 and 4 years, and possibly as old as age 5 years, some investigators have recommended that radiation therapy be delayed or omitted entirely. Such decisions are based on multiple factors, including histological subtype, molecular subtype, extent of disease at diagnosis, preexisting neurological and neurodevelopmental status, and family preferences. Most long-term survivors who have been successfully treated with chemotherapy alone have had nondisseminated completely resected tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_2.79">

295679</a>

2957,<a href="#cit/section_2.101">

2958101</a>

2959,<a href="#cit/section_2.103">

2960103</a>

2961]; [<a href="#cit/section_2.104">

2962104</a>

2963][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2964Level of evidence B4</a>

2965] Several studies that have used chemotherapy alone for younger patients have observed that the finding of desmoplasia (seen in patients with desmoplastic medulloblastoma or MBEN) and/or molecular evidence of SHH signaling connotes a significantly better prognosis than does the finding of classic or large cell/anaplastic medulloblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_2.7">

29667</a>

2967,<a href="#cit/section_2.26">

296826</a>

2969,<a href="#cit/section_2.64">

297064</a>

2971-<a href="#cit/section_2.66">

297266</a>

2973]; [<a href="#cit/section_2.80">

297480</a>

2975][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2976Level of evidence B4</a>

2977]</p>

2978<p id="_1919" tabindex="-1">

2979The treatment of younger children with newly diagnosed medulloblastoma continues to evolve. Results have been variable, and comparison across studies has been difficult because of differences in the drug regimens used and the utilization of craniospinal and local boost radiation therapy at the end of chemotherapy or when children reached age 3 years in some studies.</p>

2980<p id="_1920" tabindex="-1">

2981Standard treatment options for younger children with newly diagnosed medulloblastoma include the following:</p>

2982<div class="pdq-content-list">

2983<ol id="_1929">

2984<li>

2985<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-cns-embryonal-treatment-pdq#_1917">

2986Surgery</a>

2987.</li>

2988<li>

2989<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-cns-embryonal-treatment-pdq#_1995">

2990Adjuvant chemotherapy</a>

2991.</li>

2992</ol>

2993</div>

2994<section id="_1917">

2995<h5 id="_1917_toc">

2996Surgery</h5>

2997<p id="_1923" tabindex="-1">

2998If feasible, complete surgical resection of the tumor is the optimal treatment. Surgical resectability is associated with histology, as patients with desmoplastic/nodular medulloblastoma or MBEN have a higher rate of complete resection than do patients with classic medulloblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_2.65">

299965</a>

3000,<a href="#cit/section_2.66">

300166</a>

3002]</p>

3003</section>

3004<section id="_1995">

3005<h5 id="_1995_toc">

3006Adjuvant chemotherapy</h5>

3007<p id="_1996" tabindex="-1">

3008Therapies for younger children with medulloblastoma have included the use of multiagent chemotherapeutic approaches, including drugs such as cyclophosphamide, etoposide, cisplatin, and vincristine, with or without concomitant high-dose intravenous and/or intraventricular methotrexate.[<a href="#cit/section_2.66">

300966</a>

3010,<a href="#cit/section_2.79">

301179</a>

3012,<a href="#cit/section_2.101">

3013101</a>

3014,<a href="#cit/section_2.103">

3015103</a>

3016,<a href="#cit/section_2.105">

3017105</a>

3018,<a href="#cit/section_2.106">

3019106</a>

3020]; [<a href="#cit/section_2.107">

3021107</a>

3022,<a href="#cit/section_2.108">

3023108</a>

3024][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3025Level of evidence B4</a>

3026] The efficacy of chemotherapy has varied depending on the histology and/or molecular subtype of the tumor.</p>

3027<section id="_2059">

3028<h6 id="_2059_toc">

3029Desmoplastic/MBEN medulloblastoma and/or tumors with SHH signaling</h6>

3030<p id="_2060" tabindex="-1">

3031A series of studies have demonstrated that intensive chemotherapy, including either high-dose systemic and intraventricular methotrexate or high-dose chemotherapy supported by stem cell rescue, without radiation therapy, is an effective treatment for most infants and very young children with medulloblastoma.</p>

3032<p id="_2061" tabindex="-1">

3033Evidence (chemotherapy):</p>

3034<div class="pdq-content-list">

3035<ol id="_2062">

3036<li>

3037In the German Hirntumor (HIT) 2000 multicenter trial, a multiagent chemotherapy regimen that included high-dose intravenous and intraventricular methotrexate was used. This drug regimen did not include high-dose chemotherapy supported by bone marrow or peripheral stem cell rescue.[<a href="#cit/section_2.79">

303879</a>

3039] <div class="pdq-content-list">

3040<ul id="_2067">

3041<li>

3042Nineteen patients with desmoplastic medulloblastoma or MBEN had a 5-year EFS rate of 90% (&#177;7%) and an OS rate of 100% (&#177;0%). </li>

3043<li>

3044All patients were treated with postoperative chemotherapy alone, and no radiation was given before progression.</li>

3045</ul>

3046</div>

3047</li>

3048<li>

3049An expanded cohort of the German HIT 2000 trial included an additional 23 children with nodular desmoplasia or MBEN who were treated with the same regimen. The following results were reported:[<a href="#cit/section_2.63">

305063</a>

3051,<a href="#cit/section_2.109">

3052109</a>

3053]<div class="pdq-content-list">

3054<ul id="_2068">

3055<li>

3056Combined results confirmed the excellent survival, with a 5-year EFS rate of greater than 90%. </li>

3057<li>

3058In this expanded cohort, molecular characterization was performed and a subset of tumors with SHH signaling were identified. These patients with tumors demonstrating SHH signaling had a similar excellent prognosis.</li>

3059<li>

3060Further characterization of the SHH signaling molecular subtype by chromosomal aberrations did not identify any differences in EFS or OS.</li>

3061<li>

3062Other studies have suggested that further subdivision by chromosomal aberrations in young children with SHH-driven medulloblastoma was predictive of outcome.</li>

3063</ul>

3064</div>

3065</li>

3066<li>

3067A COG clinical trial (CCG-9921) also observed a favorable outcome for children with desmoplastic medulloblastoma (including MBEN). In this study, patients with desmoplastic tumors did not receive radiation therapy before progression.[<a href="#cit/section_2.101">

3068101</a>

3069] <div class="pdq-content-list">

3070<ul id="_2069">

3071<li>

3072Patients in the desmoplastic group achieved an EFS rate of 77% (&#177;9%) and an OS rate of 85% (&#177;8%), compared with an EFS rate of 17% (&#177;5%) and an OS rate of 29% (&#177;6%) for patients in the nondesmoplastic group (<em>

3073P</em>

3074 &lt; .0001 for both EFS and OS comparisons).</li>

3075</ul>

3076</div>

3077</li>

3078<li>

3079The <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT02017964">

3080COG-ACNS1221 (NCT02017964)</a>

3081 study tested systemic chemotherapy that was identical to the chemotherapy used in the German HIT 2000 trial, except for the omission of intraventricular methotrexate.[<a href="#cit/section_2.27">

308227</a>

3083]<div class="pdq-content-list">

3084<ul id="_2070">

3085<li>

3086The study was closed early because of a higher-than-expected rate of relapse, with a 2-year PFS rate of 52% in the 25 patients who were studied.</li>

3087</ul>

3088</div>

3089</li>

3090<li>

3091Another treatment option for children younger than 3 years at diagnosis is high-dose chemotherapy. Results of trials using higher-dose, marrow-ablative chemotherapeutic regimens supported by stem cell rescue have also demonstrated that a subgroup of patients with medulloblastoma who are younger than 3 years and, in some studies, younger than 5 years at the time of diagnosis can be successfully treated with chemotherapy alone.[<a href="#cit/section_2.102">

3092102</a>

3093,<a href="#cit/section_2.104">

3094104</a>

3095,<a href="#cit/section_2.110">

3096110</a>

3097][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3098Level of evidence B4</a>

3099]; [<a href="#cit/section_2.111">

3100111</a>

3101] <div class="pdq-content-list">

3102<ol id="_2071" class="lower-alpha">

3103<li>

3104The best survival rates using this higher-dose chemotherapy approach have been seen in patients with desmoplastic medulloblastoma and MBEN.[<a href="#cit/section_2.111">

3105111</a>

3106]<div class="pdq-content-list">

3107<ul id="_2072">

3108<li>

3109After treatment with chemotherapy without concomitant radiation therapy, patients with nondisseminated disease have achieved survival rates of nearly 90%, and patients with disseminated disease have achieved survival rates of 80%.</li>

3110</ul>

3111</div>

3112</li>

3113</ol>

3114</div>

3115</li>

3116<li>

3117One study reported the outcomes of infants and young children with relapsed medulloblastoma who were initially treated without craniospinal irradiation (CSI).[<a href="#cit/section_2.112">

3118112</a>

3119] <div class="pdq-content-list">

3120<ul id="_2130">

3121<li>

3122 A substantial portion of these children were treated with CSI-based regimens and their disease was successfully salvaged. </li>

3123<li>

3124The 3-year postrelapse survival rate was 52.4% for patients treated with curative intent. </li>

3125<li>

3126The report found that older age at diagnosis, local relapse, and the SHH infant medulloblastoma subtype were associated with better postrelapse survival. </li>

3127<li>

3128The addition of chemotherapy to CSI did not improve outcomes.</li>

3129</ul>

3130</div>

3131</li>

3132</ol>

3133</div>

3134</section>

3135<section id="_2063">

3136<h6 id="_2063_toc">

3137Nondesmoplastic, non-MBEN, and non-SHH signaling&#8211;driven medulloblastoma</h6>

3138<p id="_2066" tabindex="-1">

3139Compared with children with desmoplastic medulloblastoma or MBEN treated with current intensive chemotherapy regimens, children with other histological and/or molecular subtypes fare less well. One study suggested that patients with molecularly identified group 4 tumors did well with chemotherapy alone.[<a href="#cit/section_2.109">

3140109</a>

3141]</p>

3142<p id="_2064" tabindex="-1">

3143Evidence (chemotherapy):</p>

3144<div class="pdq-content-list">

3145<ol id="_2065">

3146<li>

3147In children with nondesmoplastic, non-MBEN, and/or non-SHH&#8211;signaling tumors, the EFS rates are less than 40% despite the use of intensive chemotherapy supplemented with methotrexate (intravenous and intraventricular) or the use of high-dose chemotherapy regimens supported with stem cell rescue.[<a href="#cit/section_2.66">

314866</a>

3149,<a href="#cit/section_2.101">

3150101</a>

3151,<a href="#cit/section_2.109">

3152109</a>

3153,<a href="#cit/section_2.111">

3154111</a>

3155,<a href="#cit/section_2.113">

3156113</a>

3157] <div class="pdq-content-list">

3158<ul id="_2073">

3159<li>

3160Outcome is particularly poor when these patients have disseminated disease. </li>

3161</ul>

3162</div>

3163</li>

3164<li>

3165In some studies, radiation therapy to the primary tumor site and/or craniospinal axis has been added after chemotherapy, which makes the assessment of the efficacy of chemotherapy more difficult.[<a href="#cit/section_2.109">

3166109</a>

3167,<a href="#cit/section_2.111">

3168111</a>

3169,<a href="#cit/section_2.113">

3170113</a>

3171]</li>

3172</ol>

3173</div>

3174<p id="_2129" tabindex="-1">

3175There is no consensus on how much radiation therapy (dose and extent) should be given and at what age radiation therapy should be instituted in young patients with localized or disseminated disease. </p>

3176<div class="pdq-content-list">

3177<ul id="_2074">

3178<li>

3179In the expanded HIT 2000 study, the addition of focal radiation therapy to the primary tumor site in patients with localized disease after chemotherapy did not improve EFS or OS.[<a href="#cit/section_2.109">

3180109</a>

3181]</li>

3182<li>

3183In the St. Jude Children's Research Hospital <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00602667">

3184SJYC07 (NCT00602667)</a>

3185 study, focal radiation therapy also did not improve EFS in infants with medulloblastoma denoted as intermediate risk (5-year EFS rate, 25% &#177; 12%).[<a href="#cit/section_2.63">

318663</a>

3187]</li>

3188<li>

3189The COG <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00006461">

3190P9934 (NCT00006461)</a>

3191 study, which also employed focal radiation therapy, had a similar EFS (4-year EFS rate, 23% &#177; 12%) for patients with nondesmoplastic medulloblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_2.114">

3192114</a>

3193]</li>

3194<li>

3195In the St Jude SJYC07 study, 29 of the 54 infants with medulloblastoma who progressed received craniospinal irradiation (median dose, 36 Gy). Of the 29 patients, 18 (62%) survived, compared with 6 (24%) of 25 patients who did not receive craniospinal irradiation.[<a href="#cit/section_2.63">

319663</a>

3197]</li>

3198</ul>

3199</div>

3200</section>

3201</section>

3202</section>

3203<section id="_1928">

3204<h4 id="_1928_toc">

3205Treatment of children older than 3 years with average-risk medulloblastoma</h4>

3206<p id="_1931" tabindex="-1">

3207Standard treatment options for children older than 3 years with newly diagnosed average-risk medulloblastoma include the following:</p>

3208<div class="pdq-content-list">

3209<ol id="_1932">

3210<li>

3211<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-cns-embryonal-treatment-pdq#_1930">

3212Surgery</a>

3213.</li>

3214<li>

3215<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-cns-embryonal-treatment-pdq#_1936">

3216Adjuvant radiation therapy</a>

3217.</li>

3218<li>

3219<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-cns-embryonal-treatment-pdq#_1941">

3220Adjuvant chemotherapy</a>

3221.</li>

3222</ol>

3223</div>

3224<section id="_1930">

3225<h5 id="_1930_toc">

3226Surgery</h5>

3227<p id="_1935" tabindex="-1">

3228If feasible, total or near-total removal of the tumor is considered optimal.[<a href="#cit/section_2.82">

322982</a>

3230]</p>

3231</section>

3232<section id="_1936">

3233<h5 id="_1936_toc">

3234Adjuvant radiation therapy</h5>

3235<p id="_1937" tabindex="-1">

3236Radiation therapy is usually initiated after surgery with or without concurrent chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_2.115">

3237115</a>

3238-<a href="#cit/section_2.117">

3239117</a>

3240] The best survival results for children with medulloblastoma have been obtained when radiation therapy is initiated within 4 to 6 weeks postsurgery.[<a href="#cit/section_2.116">

3241116</a>

3242-<a href="#cit/section_2.118">

3243118</a>

3244]; [<a href="#cit/section_2.92">

324592</a>

3246,<a href="#cit/section_2.119">

3247119</a>

3248][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3249Level of evidence A1</a>

3250]</p>

3251<p id="_2028" tabindex="-1">

3252The radiation dose for patients with average-risk medulloblastoma is 54 Gy to the posterior fossa or local tumor bed and 23.4 Gy to the entire neuraxis (i.e., the whole brain and spine), termed craniospinal irradiation.[<a href="#cit/section_2.115">

3253115</a>

3254-<a href="#cit/section_2.117">

3255117</a>

3256,<a href="#cit/section_2.120">

3257120</a>

3258]</p>

3259<p id="_1999" tabindex="-1">

3260Evidence (adjuvant radiation therapy):</p>

3261<div class="pdq-content-list">

3262<ol id="_2000">

3263<li>

3264With radiation therapy alone, using a craniospinal radiation dose of 35 Gy with a boost to the posterior fossa of 55 Gy, 5-year EFS rates range between 50% and 65% in patients with nondisseminated disease.[<a href="#cit/section_2.78">

326578</a>

3266,<a href="#cit/section_2.116">

3267116</a>

3268]</li>

3269<li>

3270The minimal dose of craniospinal radiation needed for disease control is unknown. Attempts to lower the dose of craniospinal radiation therapy to 23.4 Gy without chemotherapy have resulted in an increased incidence of isolated leptomeningeal relapse.[<a href="#cit/section_2.120">

3271120</a>

3272] <p id="_2001" tabindex="-1">

3273Lower doses and boost volume of craniospinal radiation were evaluated in a COG study (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00085735">

3274NCT00085735</a>

3275). Children aged 3 to 7 years were randomly assigned to receive a craniospinal radiation dose of either 18 Gy or 23.4 Gy, as well as whole posterior fossa versus limited target volume boost to the tumor bed.[<a href="#cit/section_2.58">

327658</a>

3277][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3278Level of evidence A1</a>

3279]</p>

3280<div class="pdq-content-list">

3281<ul id="_2002">

3282<li>

3283Results revealed that 18 Gy of craniospinal irradiation was inferior to 23.4 Gy of craniospinal irradiation (5-year EFS rate of 82.6% &#177; 4.2% and OS rate of 85.8% for patients who received 23.4 Gy vs. EFS rate of 71.9% &#177; 4.9% and OS rate of 77.9% &#177; 4.9% for patients who received 18 Gy).</li>

3284<li>

3285The 5-year EFS rate was 82.5% for patients who received radiation therapy targeting the tumor bed, compared with 80.5% for patients who received posterior fossa radiation therapy. Therefore, radiation therapy targeting the tumor bed was not inferior to posterior fossa radiation therapy (hazard ratio, 0.97; 94% upper confidence interval, 1.32). </li>

3286</ul>

3287</div>

3288<p id="_2003" tabindex="-1">

3289Analysis according to molecular subgroups demonstrated that children with group 4 medulloblastoma who received 18 Gy of craniospinal radiation therapy had poorer EFS than those who received 23.4 Gy. This was not demonstrated in the other molecular subgroups, although the study was not sized for molecular subgroup analysis.[<a href="#cit/section_2.58">

329058</a>

3291] Craniospinal radiation dose reduction to 18 Gy is currently being investigated in WNT medulloblastoma patients (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT02724579">

3292NCT02724579</a>

3293), the molecular subgroup with the best prognosis.</p>

3294</li>

3295<li>

3296The <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT01351870">

3297SIOP-PNET-4 (NCT01351870)</a>

3298 study compared daily radiation therapy (1.8 Gy fractions with 23.4 Gy to the neuraxis and a 30 Gy boost to the posterior fossa) with twice-per-day radiation (1 Gy fractions with 36 Gy and a 24-Gy boost to the posterior fossa).[<a href="#cit/section_2.121">

3299121</a>

3300]<div class="pdq-content-list">

3301<ul id="_2004">

3302<li>

3303With a median follow-up of 7.8 years, the 10-year OS was not significantly different between the two radiation groups.</li>

3304<li>

3305Long-term side effects were not reported in this study.</li>

3306</ul>

3307</div>

3308</li>

3309<li>

3310If chemotherapy is added after radiation therapy, 23.4 Gy of craniospinal radiation therapy has been shown to be an effective dose.[<a href="#cit/section_2.92">

331192</a>

3312,<a href="#cit/section_2.121">

3313121</a>

3314-<a href="#cit/section_2.123">

3315123</a>

3316] Lower doses are being evaluated.</li>

3317<li>

3318Although the standard boost in medulloblastoma is to the entire posterior fossa, failure data patterns reveal that radiation therapy to the tumor bed instead of the entire posterior fossa is equally effective and may be associated with reduced toxicity.[<a href="#cit/section_2.124">

3319124</a>

3320,<a href="#cit/section_2.125">

3321125</a>

3322]; [<a href="#cit/section_2.58">

332358</a>

3324][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3325Level of evidence A1</a>

3326] </li>

3327</ol>

3328</div>

3329</section>

3330<section id="_1941">

3331<h5 id="_1941_toc">

3332Adjuvant chemotherapy</h5>

3333<p id="_1942" tabindex="-1">

3334Chemotherapy is now a standard component of the treatment of children with average-risk medulloblastoma.</p>

3335<p id="_2005" tabindex="-1">

3336Evidence (adjuvant chemotherapy):</p>

3337<div class="pdq-content-list">

3338<ol id="_1943">

3339<li>

3340Prospective randomized trials and single-arm trials suggest that adjuvant chemotherapy given during and after radiation therapy improves OS for children with average-risk medulloblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_2.89">

334189</a>

3342,<a href="#cit/section_2.115">

3343115</a>

3344-<a href="#cit/section_2.119">

3345119</a>

3346]<div class="pdq-content-list">

3347<ul id="_1944">

3348<li>

3349Radiation therapy and chemotherapy given during and after surgery has demonstrated 5-year EFS rates of 70% to 85%.[<a href="#cit/section_2.115">

3350115</a>

3351-<a href="#cit/section_2.117">

3352117</a>

3353]; [<a href="#cit/section_2.126">

3354126</a>

3355][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3356Level of evidence B4</a>

3357]</li>

3358</ul>

3359</div>

3360</li>

3361<li>

3362A lower radiation dose of 23.4 Gy to the neuraxis when coupled with chemotherapy has been shown to result in disease control in up to 85% of patients and may decrease the severity of long-term neurocognitive sequelae.[<a href="#cit/section_2.92">

336392</a>

3364,<a href="#cit/section_2.122">

3365122</a>

3366,<a href="#cit/section_2.123">

3367123</a>

3368,<a href="#cit/section_2.127">

3369127</a>

3370]</li>

3371<li>

3372A variety of chemotherapeutic regimens have been successfully used, including the combination of cisplatin, lomustine, and vincristine or the combination of cisplatin, cyclophosphamide, and vincristine.[<a href="#cit/section_2.115">

3373115</a>

3374,<a href="#cit/section_2.116">

3375116</a>

3376,<a href="#cit/section_2.127">

3377127</a>

3378,<a href="#cit/section_2.128">

3379128</a>

3380] <div class="pdq-content-list">

3381<ul id="_2125">

3382<li>

3383 These therapies have increased 5-year and 10-year EFS and OS rates and have likely decreased the incidence of late relapse. </li>

3384<li>

3385However, long-term survivors treated with multimodality therapy are at a high risk of late effects such as hearing loss, cardiac complications, and secondary neoplasms.[<a href="#cit/section_2.129">

3386129</a>

3387]</li>

3388</ul>

3389</div>

3390<p id="_2006" tabindex="-1">

3391 In addition, postradiation high-dose cyclophosphamide supported by peripheral stem cell rescue, but with reduced cumulative doses of vincristine and cisplatin, has resulted in similar survival rates.[<a href="#cit/section_2.57">

339257</a>

3393]</p>

3394</li>

3395<li>

3396Although medulloblastoma is often sensitive to chemotherapy, preradiation chemotherapy has not been shown to improve survival in patients with average-risk medulloblastoma, compared with radiation therapy and subsequent chemotherapy. In some prospective studies, preradiation chemotherapy has been related to a poorer rate of survival.[<a href="#cit/section_2.116">

3397116</a>

3398-<a href="#cit/section_2.119">

3399119</a>

3400]</li>

3401</ol>

3402</div>

3403</section>

3404</section>

3405<section id="_38">

3406<h4 id="_38_toc">

3407Treatment of children older than 3 years with high-risk medulloblastoma</h4>

3408<p id="_478" tabindex="-1">

3409Standard treatment options for children older than 3 years who are newly diagnosed with medulloblastoma and have metastatic disease or have had a subtotal resection include the following:</p>

3410<div class="pdq-content-list">

3411<ol id="_479">

3412<li>

3413<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-cns-embryonal-treatment-pdq#_39">

3414Surgery</a>

3415.</li>

3416<li>

3417<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-cns-embryonal-treatment-pdq#_482">

3418Adjuvant radiation therapy</a>

3419.</li>

3420<li>

3421<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-cns-embryonal-treatment-pdq#_649">

3422Adjuvant chemotherapy</a>

3423.</li>

3424</ol>

3425</div>

3426<p id="_2007" tabindex="-1">

3427In high-risk patients, numerous studies have demonstrated that multimodality therapy improves the duration of disease control and overall disease-free survival.[<a href="#cit/section_2.57">

342857</a>

3429,<a href="#cit/section_2.130">

3430130</a>

3431] Studies show that approximately 50% to 70% of patients with high-risk disease, including those with metastatic disease, will experience long-term disease control.[<a href="#cit/section_2.57">

343257</a>

3433,<a href="#cit/section_2.115">

3434115</a>

3435,<a href="#cit/section_2.130">

3436130</a>

3437-<a href="#cit/section_2.134">

3438134</a>

3439]; [<a href="#cit/section_2.135">

3440135</a>

3441,<a href="#cit/section_2.136">

3442136</a>

3443][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3444Level of evidence A1</a>

3445]; [<a href="#cit/section_2.137">

3446137</a>

3447][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3448Level of evidence B4</a>

3449] A completed COG study demonstrated that children with group 3 <em class="gene-name">

3450MYC</em>

3451-amplified tumors who were randomized to receive carboplatin during radiation therapy had improved 5-year EFS and OS rates, compared with those who did not receive carboplatin concurrently with radiation therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_2.131">

3452131</a>

3453]</p>

3454<section id="_39">

3455<h5 id="_39_toc">

3456Surgery</h5>

3457<p id="_481" tabindex="-1">

3458Treatment for high-risk patients is the same as for those with average-risk disease. An attempt at gross-total resection is considered optimal, if feasible.[<a href="#cit/section_2.78">

345978</a>

3460,<a href="#cit/section_2.82">

346182</a>

3462]</p>

3463</section>

3464<section id="_482">

3465<h5 id="_482_toc">

3466Adjuvant radiation therapy</h5>

3467<p id="_2008" tabindex="-1">

3468In contrast to standard-risk treatment, the craniospinal radiation dose is generally 36 Gy. </p>

3469</section>

3470<section id="_649">

3471<h5 id="_649_toc">

3472Adjuvant chemotherapy</h5>

3473<p id="_2009" tabindex="-1">

3474Evidence (adjuvant chemotherapy):</p>

3475<div class="pdq-content-list">

3476<ol id="_487">

3477<li>

3478The drugs that are useful in children with average-risk disease are the same drugs that have been used extensively in children with high-risk disease, including cisplatin, lomustine, cyclophosphamide, etoposide, and vincristine.[<a href="#cit/section_2.135">

3479135</a>

3480]<div class="pdq-content-list">

3481<ul id="_2126">

3482<li>

3483 These therapies have increased 5-year and 10-year EFS and OS rates and have likely decreased the incidence of late relapse. </li>

3484<li>

3485However, long-term survivors treated with multimodality therapy are at a high risk of late effects such as hearing loss, cardiac complications, and secondary neoplasms.[<a href="#cit/section_2.129">

3486129</a>

3487]</li>

3488</ul>

3489</div>

3490</li>

3491<li>

3492Postradiation high-dose nonmyeloablative chemotherapy supported by peripheral stem cell rescue, but with reduced cumulative doses of vincristine and cisplatin, has also been used and has resulted in 5-year PFS rates of approximately 60%.[<a href="#cit/section_2.57">

349357</a>

3494]</li>

3495<li>

3496The COG <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00392327">

3497ACNS0332 (NCT00392327)</a>

3498 study randomly assigned patients to receive daily carboplatin compared with weekly vincristine during radiation therapy (36 Gy craniospinal plus a posterior fossa boost) followed by six cycles of adjuvant treatment with cisplatin, cyclophosphamide, and vincristine.[<a href="#cit/section_2.131">

3499131</a>

3500]<div class="pdq-content-list">

3501<ul id="_2099">

3502<li>

3503Of the 261 evaluable patients, the 5-year EFS rate was 62.9%, and the OS rate was 73.4%.</li>

3504<li>

3505For the entire cohort, there was no difference in EFS rate for patients treated with carboplatin (66.4%) than for patients who were not treated with carboplatin (59.2%).</li>

3506<li>

3507In a subset analysis based on molecular subgrouping, patients with group 3 medulloblastoma appeared to benefit from the use of daily carboplatin during radiation therapy, with a 5-year EFS rate of 73.2% for patients who received carboplatin, compared with 53.7% for those who did not.</li>

3508<li>

3509A second randomization testing the utility of isotretinoin maintenance therapy was closed at the time of a planned interim analysis for futility.</li>

3510</ul>

3511</div>

3512</li>

3513<li>

3514In a trial of 51 patients with newly diagnosed high-risk disease, patients were treated with postoperative induction chemotherapy (etoposide and carboplatin) followed by two high-dose thiotepa courses with peripheral stem cell rescue and risk-adapted craniospinal radiation therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_2.132">

3515132</a>

3516]<div class="pdq-content-list">

3517<ul id="_2127">

3518<li>

3519The 5-year PFS and OS rates were 76%.</li>

3520</ul>

3521</div>

3522</li>

3523</ol>

3524</div>

3525</section>

3526</section>

3527<section id="_42">

3528<h4 id="_42_toc">

3529Treatment options under clinical evaluation for childhood medulloblastoma</h4>

3530<p id="_272" tabindex="-1">

3531Early-phase therapeutic trials may be available for selected patients. These trials may be available via the <a href="https://www.childrensoncologygroup.org/" title="https://www.childrensoncologygroup.org/">

3532COG</a>

3533, the <a href="https://www.pbtc.org/" title="https://www.pbtc.org/">

3534Pediatric Brain Tumor Consortium</a>

3535, or other entities. Information about National Cancer Institute (NCI)&#8211;supported clinical trials can be found on the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search">

3536NCI website</a>

3537. For information about clinical trials sponsored by other organizations, see the <a href="https://clinicaltrials.gov/" title="https://clinicaltrials.gov/">

3538ClinicalTrials.gov website</a>

3539.</p>

3540<p id="_1812" tabindex="-1">

3541The following is an example of a national and/or institutional clinical trial that is currently being conducted:</p>

3542<div class="pdq-content-list">

3543<ul id="_1866">

3544<li>

3545<strong>

3546<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT02724579">

3547COG-ACNS1422 (NCT02724579)</a>

3548</strong>

3549 (Reduced Craniospinal Radiation Therapy and Chemotherapy in Treating Younger Patients With Newly Diagnosed WNT-Driven Medulloblastoma)<strong>

3550: </strong>

3551This phase II trial studies the effectiveness of reduced doses of radiation therapy to the brain and spine (craniospinal) and chemotherapy for patients with the newly diagnosed type of brain tumor called WNT/Wingless (WNT)&#8211;driven medulloblastoma. Studies using chemotherapy and radiation therapy are shown to be effective in treating patients with WNT-driven medulloblastoma.</li>

3552</ul>

3553</div>

3554</section>

3555</section>

4112 <section id="_1945">

4113

4114 <h2>

4115Childhood Nonmedulloblastoma Embryonal Tumors</h2>

4116

4117 <div id="_section_1945" class="pdq-sections">

4118<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

4119<h6>

4120In This Section</h6>

4121<ul>

4122<li>

4123<a href="#_1946_toc">

4124Clinical Presentation</a>

4125</li>

4126<li>

4127<a href="#_1949_toc">

4128Cellular and Molecular Classification<ul>

4129<li>

4130<a href="#_1950_toc">

4131Subtypes of nonmedulloblastoma embryonal tumors</a>

4132</li>

4133</ul>

4134</a>

4135</li>

4136<li>

4137<a href="#_1948_toc">

4138Staging Evaluation</a>

4139</li>

4140<li>

4141<a href="#_1953_toc">

4142Treatment of Childhood Nonmedulloblastoma Embryonal Tumors<ul>

4143<li>

4144<a href="#_1954_toc">

4145Treatment of children aged 3 years and younger</a>

4146</li>

4147<li>

4148<a href="#_1956_toc">

4149Treatment of children older than 3 years</a>

4150</li>

4151</ul>

4152</a>

4153</li>

4154<li>

4155<a href="#_1962_toc">

4156Treatment of Childhood Embryonal Tumors With Multilayered Rosettes or Medulloepithelioma</a>

4157</li>

4158<li>

4159<a href="#_2115_toc">

4160Treatment of CNS Neuroblastoma, <em class="gene-name">

4161FOXR2</em>

4162-activated</a>

4163</li>

4164</ul>

4165</nav>

4166<p id="_2034" tabindex="-1">

4167The 2021 World Health Organization (WHO) Classification of Tumors of the Central Nervous System (CNS) is listed below in the <a href="/types/brain/hp/child-cns-embryonal-treatment-pdq#_1949">

4168Cellular and Molecular Classification</a>

4169 section. All nonmedulloblastoma tumors of neuroectodermal origin that lack the specific histopathological features or molecular alterations that define other CNS tumors are classified as CNS embryonal tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_3.1">

41701</a>

4171,<a href="#cit/section_3.2">

41722</a>

4173] These tumors will be discussed in this section, with the exception of atypical teratoid/rhabdoid tumors (AT/RTs). For more information, see <a href="/types/brain/hp/child-cns-atrt-treatment-pdq">

4174Childhood CNS AT/RT Treatment</a>

4175. Pineoblastoma will also be discussed in this summary because it shares histological features with the embryonal tumors and is conventionally treated in the same fashion. For more information, see the <a href="/types/brain/hp/child-cns-embryonal-treatment-pdq#_1963">

4176Childhood Pineoblastoma</a>

4177 section.</p>

4178<section id="_1946">

4179<h3 id="_1946_toc">

4180Clinical Presentation</h3>

4181<p id="_1947" tabindex="-1">

4182For nonmedulloblastoma embryonal tumors, presentation is also relatively rapid and depends on the location of the tumor in the nervous system. Embryonal tumors tend to grow fast and are usually diagnosed within 3 months of initial onset of symptoms. </p>

4183<p id="_2010" tabindex="-1">

4184Nonmedulloblastoma embryonal tumors may occur anywhere in the CNS, and presentation is variable. Usually there is significant neurological dysfunction associated with lethargy and vomiting. Supratentorial embryonal tumors (see <a href="/types/brain/hp/child-cns-embryonal-treatment-pdq#_549">

4185Figure 1</a>

4186) result in focal neurological deficits such as hemiparesis and visual field loss, depending on which portion of the cerebral cortex is involved. They may also result in seizures and obtundation. </p>

4187</section>

4188<section id="_1949">

4189<h3 id="_1949_toc">

4190Cellular and Molecular Classification</h3>

4191<p id="_748" tabindex="-1">

4192The 2021 WHO Classification of Tumours of the CNS classifies nonmedulloblastoma embryonal tumors primarily by histological and immunohistological features and, in some cases, by molecular findings. The classification includes the following:[<a href="#cit/section_3.1">

41931</a>

4194,<a href="#cit/section_3.2">

41952</a>

4196]</p>

4197<div class="pdq-content-list">

4198<ul id="_2110">

4199<li>

4200Atypical teratoid/rhabdoid tumor (AT/RT).</li>

4201<li>

4202Cribriform neuroepithelial tumor.</li>

4203<li>

4204Embryonal tumor with multilayered rosettes (ETMR).</li>

4205<li>

4206CNS neuroblastoma, <em class="gene-name">

4207FOXR2</em>

4208-activated.</li>

4209<li>

4210CNS tumor with <em class="gene-name">

4211BCOR</em>

4212 internal tandem duplication.</li>

4213<li>

4214CNS embryonal tumor, not elsewhere classified (NEC)/not otherwise specified (NOS).</li>

4215</ul>

4216</div>

4217<p id="_2111" tabindex="-1">

4218NEC is defined as a tumor not elsewhere classified. The NOS nomenclature is used for tumors that cannot be further classified. For many lesions, there are overlapping histological features, and methylation-based clustering is critical for specific diagnosis.[<a href="#cit/section_3.1">

42191</a>

4220,<a href="#cit/section_3.2">

42212</a>

4222] The contribution of DNA methylation profiling to correctly diagnose supratentorial embryonal tumors was demonstrated in a clinical trial of patients with supratentorial primitive neuroectodermal tumors of the CNS (CNS-PNET) and pineoblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_3.3">

42233</a>

4224] For the pineoblastoma cases, there was high concordance between the diagnosis made by methylation profiling and the diagnosis made by central pathology review diagnosis (26 of 29). However, for the remaining 31 nonpineoblastoma patients in the study, the diagnosis made by methylation profiling was high-grade glioma in 18 patients, AT/RT in 2 patients, and <em class="gene-name">

4225RELA</em>

4226 fusion&#8211;positive ependymoma in 2 patients. Adjudication of discrepancies between the diagnosis made by central review pathology and the diagnosis made by methylation profiling was in favor of methylation profiling in the ten cases that were re-examined.</p>

4227<section id="_1950">

4228<h4 id="_1950_toc">

4229Subtypes of nonmedulloblastoma embryonal tumors</h4>

4230<section id="_sm_CDR0000779395_6">

4231<h5 id="_sm_CDR0000779395_6_toc">

4232Molecular subtypes of nonmedulloblastoma embryonal tumors</h5>

4233<p id="_sm_CDR0000779395_558" tabindex="-1">

4234Studies applying unsupervised clustering of DNA methylation patterns for nonmedulloblastoma embryonal tumors found that approximately one-half of these tumors diagnosed as nonmedulloblastoma embryonal tumors showed molecular profiles characteristic of other known pediatric brain tumors (e.g., high-grade glioma).[<a href="#cit/section_3.4">

42354</a>

4236,<a href="#cit/section_3.5">

42375</a>

4238] These observations highlight the utility of molecular characterization to assign this class of tumors to their appropriate biology-based diagnosis.</p>

4239<p id="_sm_CDR0000779395_559" tabindex="-1">

4240Among the tumors diagnosed as nonmedulloblastoma embryonal tumors, molecular characterization identified genomically and biologically distinctive subtypes, including the following:</p>

4241<div class="pdq-content-list">

4242<ul id="_sm_CDR0000779395_560">

4243<li>

4244<strong>

4245Cribriform neuroepithelial tumor:</strong>

4246 Representing less than 50% of nonmedulloblastoma embryonal tumors, this subtype is a nonrhabdoid tumor that arises in the vicinity of the third, fourth, or lateral ventricles. This tumor is characterized by cribriform strands and ribbons and demonstrates loss of nuclear SMARCB1 expression. The median age at diagnosis is 20 months. This tumor occurs more often in males, with a male-to-female ratio of 1.5 to 1.[<a href="#cit/section_3.6">

42476</a>

4248]</li>

4249<li>

4250<strong>

4251Embryonal tumor with multilayered rosettes (ETMR):</strong>

4252 Representing up to 20% of nonmedulloblastoma embryonal tumors, this subtype combines embryonal, rosette-forming, neuroepithelial brain tumors that were previously categorized as either embryonal tumor with abundant neuropil and true rosettes (ETANTR), ependymoblastoma, or medulloepithelioma.[<a href="#cit/section_3.4">

42534</a>

4254,<a href="#cit/section_3.7">

42557</a>

4256] ETMRs arise most commonly in young children (median age at diagnosis, 2&#8211;3 years) but may occur in older children.[<a href="#cit/section_3.5">

42575</a>

4258,<a href="#cit/section_3.7">

42597</a>

4260-<a href="#cit/section_3.11">

426111</a>

4262] <p id="_sm_CDR0000779395_561" tabindex="-1">

4263ETMRs are defined at the molecular level by high-level amplification of the microRNA cluster C19MC and by a gene fusion between <em class="gene-name">

4264TTYH1</em>

4265 and <em class="gene-name">

4266C19MC</em>

4267.[<a href="#cit/section_3.7">

42687</a>

4269,<a href="#cit/section_3.12">

427012</a>

4271,<a href="#cit/section_3.13">

427213</a>

4273] This gene fusion puts expression of C19MC under control of the <em class="gene-name">

4274TTYH1</em>

4275 promoter, leading to high-level aberrant expression of the microRNAs within the cluster. The World Health Organization (WHO) allows histologically similar tumors without <em class="gene-name">

4276C19MC</em>

4277 alteration to be classified as ETMR, not otherwise specified (NOS). This subclass of tumors without <em class="gene-name">

4278C19MC</em>

4279 alterations may harbor biallelic <em class="gene-name">

4280DICER1</em>

4281 mutations.</p>

4282</li>

4283<li>

4284<strong>

4285Central nervous system (CNS) neuroblastoma with FOXR2 activation (CNS NB-FOXR2):</strong>

4286 Representing 10% to 15% of nonmedulloblastoma embryonal tumors, this tumor may occur in children younger than 3 years, but it more frequently occurs in older children. This subtype is characterized by genomic alterations that lead to increased expression of the transcription factor FOXR2.[<a href="#cit/section_3.4">

42874</a>

4288] CNS NB-FOXR2 is primarily observed in children younger than 10 years, and the histology of these tumors is typically that of CNS neuroblastoma or CNS ganglioneuroblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_3.4">

42894</a>

4290,<a href="#cit/section_3.14">

429114</a>

4292] There is no single genomic alteration among CNS NB-FOXR2 tumors leading to FOXR2 overexpression, with gene fusions involving multiple <em class="gene-name">

4293FOXR2</em>

4294 partners identified.[<a href="#cit/section_3.4">

42954</a>

4296] Protein expression of SOX10 and ANKRD55 detected by immunohistochemistry has been proposed as a potential biomarker to differentiate CNS NB-FOXR2 tumors from related tumor types.[<a href="#cit/section_3.14">

429714</a>

4298]</li>

4299<li>

4300<strong>

4301CNS high-grade neuroepithelial tumor with <em class="gene-name">

4302BCOR</em>

4303 alteration (CNS HGNET-BCOR):</strong>

4304 Representing up to 3% of nonmedulloblastoma embryonal tumors, this subtype is characterized by internal tandem duplications of <em class="gene-name">

4305BCOR</em>

4306,[<a href="#cit/section_3.4">

43074</a>

4308] a genomic alteration that is also found in clear cell sarcoma of the kidney.[<a href="#cit/section_3.15">

430915</a>

4310,<a href="#cit/section_3.16">

431116</a>

4312] While the median age at diagnosis is younger than 10 years, cases arising in the second decade of life and beyond do occur.[<a href="#cit/section_3.4">

43134</a>

4314] </li>

4315</ul>

4316</div>

4317<p id="_sm_CDR0000779395_582" tabindex="-1">

4318Although not listed as separate entities in the 2021 WHO Classification of Tumours of the CNS, other nonmedulloblastoma embryonal tumors occur relatively often or are often discussed as separate entities, including the following:</p>

4319<div class="pdq-content-list">

4320<ul id="_sm_CDR0000779395_583">

4321<li>

4322<strong>

4323CNS Ewing sarcoma family tumor with <em class="gene-name">

4324CIC</em>

4325 alteration (CNS EFT-CIC):</strong>

4326 Representing 2% to 4% of nonmedulloblastoma embryonal tumors, this subtype is characterized by genomic alterations affecting <em class="gene-name">

4327CIC</em>

4328 (located on chromosome 19q13.2), with fusion to <em class="gene-name">

4329NUTM1</em>

4330 being identified in several cases tested.[<a href="#cit/section_3.4">

43314</a>

4332,<a href="#cit/section_3.5">

43335</a>

4334] <em class="gene-name">

4335CIC</em>

4336 gene fusions are also identified in extra-CNS Ewing-like sarcomas, and the gene expression signature of CNS EFT-CIC tumors is similar to that of these sarcomas.[<a href="#cit/section_3.4">

43374</a>

4338] CNS EFT-CIC tumors generally occur in children younger than 10 years and are characterized by a small cell phenotype but with variable histology.[<a href="#cit/section_3.4">

43394</a>

4340] </li>

4341<li>

4342<strong>

4343CNS high-grade neuroepithelial tumor with <em class="gene-name">

4344MN1</em>

4345 alteration (CNS HGNET-MN1):</strong>

4346 Representing 1% to 3% of nonmedulloblastoma embryonal tumors, this subtype is characterized by gene fusions involving <em class="gene-name">

4347MN1</em>

4348 (located on chromosome 22q12.3), with fusion partners including <em class="gene-name">

4349BEND2</em>

4350 and <em class="gene-name">

4351CXXC5</em>

4352.[<a href="#cit/section_3.4">

43534</a>

4354,<a href="#cit/section_3.5">

43555</a>

4356] The CNS HGNET-MN1 subtype shows a striking female predominance, and it tends to occur in the second decade of life.[<a href="#cit/section_3.4">

43574</a>

4358] This subtype contained most cases carrying a diagnosis of astroblastoma per the 2007 WHO classification scheme.[<a href="#cit/section_3.4">

43594</a>

4360] This subtype has not been added to the WHO diagnostic lexicon. Two other reports that together examined 35 cases of histologically defined astroblastoma found that 14 showed methylation profiles consistent with CNS HGNET-MN1 and/or <em class="gene-name">

4361MN1</em>

4362 alterations by fluorescence <em>

4363in situ</em>

4364 hybridization.[<a href="#cit/section_3.17">

436517</a>

4366,<a href="#cit/section_3.18">

436718</a>

4368]</li>

4369<li>

4370<strong>

4371Medulloepithelioma:</strong>

4372 Medulloepithelioma with the classic <em class="gene-name">

4373C19MC</em>

4374 amplification is considered an ETMR, <em class="gene-name">

4375C19MC</em>

4376-altered (see the ETMR information above). However, when a tumor has the histological features of medulloepithelioma, but without a <em class="gene-name">

4377C19MC</em>

4378 amplification, it is still identified as an ETMR.[<a href="#cit/section_3.19">

437919</a>

4380,<a href="#cit/section_3.20">

438120</a>

4382] Medulloepithelioma tumors are rare and tend to arise most commonly in infants and young children. Medulloepitheliomas, which histologically recapitulate the embryonal neural tube, tend to arise supratentorially, primarily intraventricularly, but may arise infratentorially, in the cauda, and even extraneurally, along nerve roots.[<a href="#cit/section_3.19">

438319</a>

4384,<a href="#cit/section_3.20">

438520</a>

4386] Intraocular medulloepithelioma is biologically distinct from intra-axial medulloepithelioma.[<a href="#cit/section_3.21">

438721</a>

4388,<a href="#cit/section_3.22">

438922</a>

4390]</li>

4391</ul>

4392</div>

4393</section>

4394</section>

4395</section>

4396<section id="_1948">

4397<h3 id="_1948_toc">

4398Staging Evaluation</h3>

4399<p id="_326" tabindex="-1">

4400Patients with nonmedulloblastoma embryonal tumors are staged in a fashion similar to that used for children with medulloblastoma. However, these patients are not assigned to average-risk and high-risk subgroups for treatment purposes because all patients are considered high risk. For more information, see the medulloblastoma <a href="/types/brain/hp/child-cns-embryonal-treatment-pdq#_1882">

4401Staging Evaluation</a>

4402 section. </p>

4403</section>

4404<section id="_1953">

4405<h3 id="_1953_toc">

4406Treatment of Childhood Nonmedulloblastoma Embryonal Tumors</h3>

4407<p id="_756" tabindex="-1">

4408For more information about the treatment of CNS AT/RTs, see <a href="/types/brain/hp/child-cns-atrt-treatment-pdq">

4409Childhood Central Nervous System Atypical Teratoid/Rhabdoid Tumor Treatment</a>

4410.</p>

4411<p id="_758" tabindex="-1">

4412For more information about the treatment of medulloepithelioma, see the <a href="/types/brain/hp/child-cns-embryonal-treatment-pdq#_1962">

4413Treatment of Childhood Embryonal Tumors With Multilayered Rosettes or Medulloepithelioma</a>

4414 section.</p>

4415<section id="_1954">

4416<h4 id="_1954_toc">

4417Treatment of children aged 3 years and younger</h4>

4418<p id="_2112" tabindex="-1">

4419The optimal treatment of childhood nonmedulloblastoma embryonal tumors remains unclear and under study. Retrospective studies of fairly large numbers of patients have suggested management approaches for the more common subgroups, including AT/RTs, ETMRs, and <em class="gene-name">

4420FOXR2</em>

4421-activated tumors. For more information about the treatment of AT/RTs, see <a href="/types/brain/hp/child-cns-atrt-treatment-pdq">

4422Childhood Central Nervous System Atypical Teratoid/Rhabdoid Tumor Treatment</a>

4423.</p>

4424<p id="_2011" tabindex="-1">

4425Standard treatment options for children aged 3 years and younger with newly diagnosed nonmedulloblastoma embryonal tumors, excluding AT/RTs, ETMRs, and <em class="gene-name">

4426FOXR2</em>

4427-activated tumors, include the following:</p>

4428<div class="pdq-content-list">

4429<ol id="_2012">

4430<li>

4431Surgery.</li>

4432<li>

4433Adjuvant chemotherapy.</li>

4434</ol>

4435</div>

4436<p id="_68" tabindex="-1">

4437Treatment of children aged 3 years and younger with embryonal tumors is similar to that outlined for children aged 3 years and younger with medulloblastoma. Aggressive surgical resection is reasonable, given the improved rate of survival for medulloblastomas and other ETMRs after total or near-total resection.[<a href="#cit/section_3.11">

443811</a>

4439] For more information, see the <a href="/types/brain/hp/child-cns-embryonal-treatment-pdq#_1916">

4440Treatment of younger children with medulloblastoma</a>

4441 section.</p>

4442<p id="_511" tabindex="-1">

4443With the use of chemotherapy alone, outcome has been variable, with survival rates at 5 years ranging between 0% and 50%.[<a href="#cit/section_3.23">

444423</a>

4445-<a href="#cit/section_3.25">

444625</a>

4447]; [<a href="#cit/section_3.26">

444826</a>

4449][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4450Level of evidence B4</a>

4451] The addition of craniospinal irradiation to chemotherapy-based regimens may successfully treat some children but with anticipated neurodevelopmental decline.[<a href="#cit/section_3.27">

445227</a>

4453][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4454Level of evidence B4</a>

4455] Localized radiation therapy to the tumor site, either before or after chemotherapy, has been given, although data supporting its efficacy are unclear.</p>

4456</section>

4457<section id="_1956">

4458<h4 id="_1956_toc">

4459Treatment of children older than 3 years</h4>

4460<p id="_501" tabindex="-1">

4461Standard treatment options for children older than 3 years with newly diagnosed nonmedulloblastoma embryonal tumors, excluding AT/RTs, ETMRs, and <em class="gene-name">

4462FOXR2</em>

4463-activated tumors, include the following:

4464

4465</p>

4466<div class="pdq-content-list">

4467<ol id="_502">

4468<li>

4469<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-cns-embryonal-treatment-pdq#_1957">

4470Surgery</a>

4471.</li>

4472<li>

4473<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-cns-embryonal-treatment-pdq#_1960">

4474Adjuvant radiation therapy.</a>

4475</li>

4476<li>

4477<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-cns-embryonal-treatment-pdq#_2014">

4478Adjuvant chemotherapy</a>

4479.</li>

4480</ol>

4481</div>

4482<section id="_1957">

4483<h5 id="_1957_toc">

4484Surgery</h5>

4485<p id="_2033" tabindex="-1">

4486Evidence (surgery): </p>

4487<div class="pdq-content-list">

4488<ol id="_1959">

4489<li>

4490Nonmedulloblastoma embryonal tumors are often amenable to resection. In reported case series, 50% to 75% of tumors in patients were totally or near-totally resected.[<a href="#cit/section_3.28">

449128</a>

4492,<a href="#cit/section_3.29">

449329</a>

4494]; [<a href="#cit/section_3.3">

44953</a>

4496][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4497Level of evidence A1</a>

4498]</li>

4499<li>

4500Attempting aggressive surgical resection is the first step in the management of newly diagnosed nonmedulloblastoma embryonal tumors. Although previous studies did not demonstrate that the extent of resection is predictive of outcome,[<a href="#cit/section_3.28">

450128</a>

4502-<a href="#cit/section_3.30">

450330</a>

4504] one study demonstrated improved survival when the tumor was completely resected.[<a href="#cit/section_3.31">

450531</a>

4506][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4507Level of evidence B4</a>

4508] A published study (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00392327">

4509COG-ACNS0332 [NCT00392327]</a>

4510) of molecularly classified nonmedulloblastoma embryonal tumors revealed improved overall survival (OS) for patients who had less than 1.5 cm<span class="sup">

45112</span>

4512 of residual disease, compared with patients who had more than 1.5 cm<span class="sup">

45132</span>

4514 of residual disease.[<a href="#cit/section_3.3">

45153</a>

4516][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4517Level of evidence A1</a>

4518]</li>

4519</ol>

4520</div>

4521</section>

4522<section id="_1960">

4523<h5 id="_1960_toc">

4524Adjuvant radiation therapy</h5>

4525<p id="_508" tabindex="-1">

4526After surgery, children with nonmedulloblastoma embryonal tumors usually receive treatment similar to that received by children with high-risk medulloblastoma.</p>

4527<p id="_2013" tabindex="-1">

4528Conventionally, patients are treated with radiation to the entire neuraxis with local boost radiation therapy, as given for medulloblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_3.30">

452930</a>

4530] Local boost radiation therapy may be problematic because of the size of the tumor and its location in the cerebral cortex. Also, there is no definitive evidence that craniospinal radiation therapy is superior to radiation to the primary tumor site alone in children with nondisseminated lesions.[<a href="#cit/section_3.28">

453128</a>

4532-<a href="#cit/section_3.30">

453330</a>

4534]</p>

4535</section>

4536<section id="_2014">

4537<h5 id="_2014_toc">

4538Adjuvant chemotherapy</h5>

4539<p id="_2015" tabindex="-1">

4540The chemotherapeutic approaches during and after radiation therapy are similar to those used for children with high-risk medulloblastoma. Three-year to 5-year OS rates of 25% to 50% have been noted.[<a href="#cit/section_3.28">

454128</a>

4542-<a href="#cit/section_3.30">

454330</a>

4544]; [<a href="#cit/section_3.31">

454531</a>

4546,<a href="#cit/section_3.32">

454732</a>

4548][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4549Level of evidence B4</a>

4550]; [<a href="#cit/section_3.33">

455133</a>

4552][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4553Level of evidence C1</a>

4554]</p>

4555<p id="_2016" tabindex="-1">

4556 In a published study of nonpineal tumors that were diagnosed as CNS primitive neuroectodermal tumors (PNETs) by traditional pathology, 71% of cases were revealed to be glioblastoma or another diagnosis by DNA methylation studies. Patients with nonmedulloblastoma embryonal tumors (n = 36) (including pineoblastomas, n = 26) had a 5-year OS rate of 78.5% (95% confidence interval [CI], 62.2%&#8211;94.8%). In contrast, the patients with glioblastoma had a 5-year OS rate of 12% (95% CI, 0%&#8211;24.7%). The study showed no benefit for children who received carboplatin or isotretinoin.[<a href="#cit/section_3.3">

45573</a>

4558][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4559Level of evidence A1</a>

4560] This study highlights the importance of molecular classification of tumors traditionally termed CNS-PNET.[<a href="#cit/section_3.4">

45614</a>

4562]</p>

4563</section>

4564</section>

4565</section>

4566<section id="_1962">

4567<h3 id="_1962_toc">

4568Treatment of Childhood Embryonal Tumors With Multilayered Rosettes or Medulloepithelioma</h3>

4569<p id="_2093" tabindex="-1">

4570A registry-based review of 159 patients with a confirmed molecular diagnosis of ETMR reported survival results for different treatments.[<a href="#cit/section_3.11">

457111</a>

4572]</p>

4573<div class="pdq-content-list">

4574<ul id="_2113">

4575<li>

4576The 2-year event-free survival (EFS) and OS rates were 0% for patients treated with conventional chemotherapy without radiation therapy, regardless of the degree of surgical resection.</li>

4577<li>

4578The 2-year EFS rate was 21% and the OS rate was 30% for patients who had a gross-total resection and were treated with high-dose chemotherapy without radiation therapy.</li>

4579<li>

4580The 2-year EFS rate was 44% for children treated with high-dose chemotherapy and radiation therapy after a subtotal resection and 66% for patients treated with high-dose chemotherapy and radiation therapy after a gross-total resection. </li>

4581<li>

4582The relative roles of focal radiation therapy versus craniospinal radiation therapy could not be assessed in this review. </li>

4583</ul>

4584</div>

4585<p id="_2114" tabindex="-1">

4586In a separate, but possibly overlapping, international retrospective review, 49 patients with histologically confirmed (by the treating institution) ETMRs were treated between 1988 to 2017 in a variety of ways.[<a href="#cit/section_3.34">

458734</a>

4588] The 5-year progression-free survival rate was 18% (&#177; 6%), and the OS rate was 24% (&#177; 6%). Most survivors received radiation therapy, including both local and craniospinal treatment, and there was no clear difference in outcomes between the types and extent of radiation therapy. The relative benefits of conventional chemotherapy compared with high-dose chemotherapy could not be assessed.[<a href="#cit/section_3.5">

45895</a>

4590]</p>

4591<p id="_2123" tabindex="-1">

4592In a subsequent publication, likely including some of the patients from the retrospective study, treatment was limited to only those who received chemotherapy and radiation therapy on a prospective P-HIT study or per the study protocol. The P-HIT study included postsurgery chemotherapy, high-dose chemotherapy, and radiation for some patients. In 35 patients with ETMRs, 8 long-term survivors were identified, 6 of whom had received either craniospinal or local radiation therapy, in addition to induction and high-dose chemotherapy. None of the patients who presented with brain stem disease survived. The 5-year survival was best for patients with localized disease, possibly for those treated with both induction and high-dose chemotherapy. The role of radiation therapy or the optimal volume of radiation therapy (local versus craniospinal) could not be determined.[<a href="#cit/section_3.10">

459310</a>

4594]</p>

4595<p id="_2124" tabindex="-1">

4596These studies suggest that the outcome for children with ETMRs may not be as dire as suggested by initial studies, which found a 5-year survival rate of 25% or lower. Outcome is more favorable in children with localized disease at the time of diagnosis and those who were treated with aggressive postsurgical chemotherapy, including induction and high-dose consolidation treatment. The role of radiation therapy is still unproven and there is no evidence that craniospinal radiation in patients with localized disease is superior to focal radiation therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_3.5">

45975</a>

4598,<a href="#cit/section_3.10">

459910</a>

4600,<a href="#cit/section_3.11">

460111</a>

4602]</p>

4603</section>

4604<section id="_2115">

4605<h3 id="_2115_toc">

4606Treatment of CNS Neuroblastoma, <em class="gene-name">

4607FOXR2</em>

4608-activated</h3>

4609<p id="_2116" tabindex="-1">

4610The optimal treatment of patients with CNS neuroblastoma, <em class="gene-name">

4611FOXR2</em>

4612-activated tumors has not been confirmed by prospective studies. In a retrospective review of patients diagnosed between 1988 and 2007, the highest rates of survival were seen after complete or near-complete resections in patients with nonmetastatic tumors who also received craniospinal radiation therapy and possibly chemotherapy. With this type of approach, up to 75% of children (35 of 42) were alive 5 years after diagnosis and treatment. This tumor tends to occur in a somewhat older population than some of the other nonmedulloblastoma embryonal tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_3.5">

46135</a>

4614] </p>

4615</section>

4760 <section id="_1963">

4761

4762 <h2>

4763Childhood Pineoblastoma</h2>

4764

4765 <div id="_section_1963" class="pdq-sections">

4766<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

4767<h6>

4768In This Section</h6>

4769<ul>

4770<li>

4771<a href="#_1964_toc">

4772Clinical Presentation</a>

4773</li>

4774<li>

4775<a href="#_1966_toc">

4776Cellular and Molecular Classification<ul>

4777<li>

4778<a href="#_sm_CDR0000799201_581_toc">

4779Genomics of Pineoblastoma</a>

4780</li>

4781</ul>

4782</a>

4783</li>

4784<li>

4785<a href="#_1965_toc">

4786Staging Evaluation</a>

4787</li>

4788<li>

4789<a href="#_1967_toc">

4790Treatment Option Overview for Childhood Pineoblastoma</a>

4791</li>

4792<li>

4793<a href="#_1968_toc">

4794Treatment of Childhood Pineoblastoma<ul>

4795<li>

4796<a href="#_1969_toc">

4797Treatment of children aged 3 years and younger</a>

4798</li>

4799<li>

4800<a href="#_1970_toc">

4801Treatment of children older than 3 years</a>

4802</li>

4803<li>

4805Treatment options under clinical evaluation for childhood pineoblastoma</a>

4806</li>

4807</ul>

4808</a>

4809</li>

4810</ul>

4811</nav>

4812<p id="_2035" tabindex="-1">

4813The World Health Organization classifies pineoblastomas in the tumors of the pineal region group. However, they are discussed in this summary because they share histological features with other embryonal tumors and are conventionally treated like other embryonal tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_4.1">

48141</a>

4815-<a href="#cit/section_4.3">

48163</a>

4817]</p>

4818<section id="_1964">

4819<h3 id="_1964_toc">

4820Clinical Presentation</h3>

4821<p id="_1975" tabindex="-1">

4822Pineoblastoma often results in hydrocephalus due to blockage of cerebrospinal fluid at the third ventricular level and other symptoms related to pressure on the back of the brain stem in the tectal region. Symptoms may include a constellation of abnormalities in eye movements (Parinaud syndrome), manifested by pupils that react poorly to light but better to accommodation, loss of upgaze, retraction or convergence nystagmus, and lid retraction. As they grow, these tumors may also cause hemiparesis and ataxia.[<a href="#cit/section_4.4">

48234</a>

4824]</p>

4825</section>

4826<section id="_1966">

4827<h3 id="_1966_toc">

4828Cellular and Molecular Classification</h3>

4829<p id="_751" tabindex="-1">

4830Pineoblastoma is histologically similar to medulloblastoma and shares histological features with embryonal tumors. It is classified as a subgroup of pineal parenchymal tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_4.5">

48315</a>

4832,<a href="#cit/section_4.6">

48336</a>

4834]</p>

4835<p id="_2077" tabindex="-1">

4836In one study of 58 children with pineoblastoma, a subclassification schema was presented, and it separated patients into four subgroups. Subgroup PB-A contained only two patients, both of whom were younger than 2 years, had tumors that were likely <em class="gene-name">

4837RB1</em>

4838 altered, and succumbed to their disease. Subgroups PB-B and PB-B-like, the largest groups, contained patients who exhibited either <em class="gene-name">

4839DICER1</em>

4840 or <em class="gene-name">

4841DROSHA</em>

4842 loss, were older, and had the best prognosis after treatment with upfront craniospinal radiation therapy. A fourth subgroup, PB-FOXR2, had five patients who were either infants or young children and had very poor rates of survival. Of the 58 patients assessed in the study, 9 were not assigned to one of the subgroups. The tumors of these nine patients clustered more closely with other types of embryonal tumors. This finding demonstrates the heterogeneity of pineal embryonal tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_4.7">

48437</a>

4844] </p>

4845<section id="_sm_CDR0000799201_581">

4846<h4 id="_sm_CDR0000799201_581_toc">

4847Genomics of Pineoblastoma</h4>

4848<p id="_sm_CDR0000799201_563" tabindex="-1">

4849Pineoblastoma, which was previously conventionally grouped with embryonal tumors, is now categorized by the World Health Organization (WHO) as a pineal parenchymal tumor. Given that therapies for pineoblastoma are quite similar to those used for embryonal tumors, the previous convention of including pineoblastoma with the central nervous system (CNS) embryonal tumors is followed here. Pineoblastoma is associated with germline mutations in both the <em class="gene-name">

4850RB1</em>

4851 gene and in <em class="gene-name">

4852DICER1</em>

4853, as described below:</p>

4854<div class="pdq-content-list">

4855<ul id="_sm_CDR0000799201_564">

4856<li>

4857Pineoblastoma is associated with germline mutations in <em class="gene-name">

4858RB1</em>

4859, with the term <em>

4860trilateral retinoblastoma</em>

4861 used to refer to ocular retinoblastoma in combination with a histologically similar brain tumor generally arising in the pineal gland or other midline structures. Historically, intracranial tumors have been reported in 5% to 15% of children with heritable retinoblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_4.8">

48628</a>

4863] Rates of pineoblastoma among children with heritable retinoblastoma who undergo current treatment programs may be lower than these historical estimates.[<a href="#cit/section_4.9">

48649</a>

4865-<a href="#cit/section_4.11">

486611</a>

4867]</li>

4868<li>

4869Germline <em class="gene-name">

4870DICER1</em>

4871 mutations have also been reported in patients with pineoblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_4.12">

487212</a>

4873] Among 18 patients with pineoblastoma, 3 patients with <em class="gene-name">

4874DICER1</em>

4875 germline mutations were identified, and an additional 3 patients known to be carriers of germline <em class="gene-name">

4876DICER1</em>

4877 mutations developed pineoblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_4.12">

487812</a>

4879] The <em class="gene-name">

4880DICER1</em>

4881 mutations in patients with pineoblastoma are loss-of-function mutations that appear to be distinct from the mutations observed in DICER1 syndrome&#8211;related tumors such as pleuropulmonary blastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_4.12">

488212</a>

4883]</li>

4884</ul>

4885</div>

4886</section>

4887</section>

4888<section id="_1965">

4889<h3 id="_1965_toc">

4890Staging Evaluation</h3>

4891<p id="_324" tabindex="-1">

4892Dissemination at the time of diagnosis occurs in 10% to 30% of patients with pineoblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_4.13">

489313</a>

4894] Because of the location of the tumor, total resections are uncommon, and most patients have only a biopsy or a subtotal resection before postsurgical treatment.[<a href="#cit/section_4.13">

489513</a>

4896,<a href="#cit/section_4.14">

489714</a>

4898] Staging for children with pineoblastomas is the same as for children with medulloblastoma. However, the patients are not assigned to average-risk and high-risk subgroups for treatment purposes.[<a href="#cit/section_4.13">

489913</a>

4900] For more information, see the medulloblastoma <a href="/types/brain/hp/child-cns-embryonal-treatment-pdq#_1882">

4901Staging Evaluation</a>

4902 section.</p>

4903</section>
